0312427999|3.0|I have had \\"The Shock Doctrine\\" sitting on my bedside table for almost two years.  I've always been meaning to read it but always put it off for other books.  I eventually bit the bullet.<br /><br />Naomi Klein writes very well.  Her prose is excellent.  The book flows well and she brings the threads of her argument together with seemingly little effort.  However, the basis of her argument is probably pushed too far. She sees disruption (eg coups, natural disasters, economic collapse, etc) as opportunities for governments to impose extreme market solutions.  In this regard, she does provide a number of good examples such as post-Pinochet Chile, Bolivia and post-Gorbachev Russia.  These disruptions provided the cover for so-called extreme economics.  Perhaps the best known is Chile where the \\"Chicago boys\\" imposed the notions of Milton Friedman directly upon an upturned country following the Pinochet coup.<br /><br />Yet while Klein argues a good point, she concludes on much weaker points.  In particular, she sees such leaders as Hugo Chavez as being counterpoints to the Chicago orthodoxy.  Not a good example.  As time has shown, Chavez is anything but a democrat and the Venezuelan economy is seriously fraying at the seams.  In other words, it is not possible to completely ignore markets.  Whether we like it or not, liberal social democracy always produces the best outcomes both in terms of standards of living and the avoidance of massive wealth discrepancies.  For some reason, Klein cannot sign on to this view.  It is here that her argument fails.<br /><br />So, overall, there is much to recommend in this book.  Klein's argument is well presented and clear.  However, her conclusions are weak.  She overdoes herself.  This is a shame.  The book has much to offer.  A poor conclusion detracts from all the previous quality research.

1607882302|5.0|Zappos went from zero to over a billion dollars in a decade that saw 9-11, the banking crisis and a couple of recessions.  The author of this book (Tony Hsieh) was one of the key initial investors in Zappos and then the CEO for most of the decade.  The story is part Tony's personal biography and part business biography, but all interesting.  The company and Tony have a tremendous focus on service & \\"happiness\\" of customers and employees.  It seems like the kind of culture you dream about working in when you think about what you really want to be when you grow up.  Zappos supported the \\"happiness\\" through careful hiring practices, deliberate unconventional policies and Tony provides many stories of specific actions that have ensured engagement, openness and growth on the employee side.  I believe many organizations will evolve to become healthier as we gain the ability to make longer term consequences visible and proceduralize our best leaders' longer-term visions, but we need more examples like Zappos to shatter old paradigms that are at best no longer useful and at worst destructive.  I'm fully aware that we're not there yet, but this book provides a cool glimpse into the future that I'll be helping to create.

Combined with weekly harangues at christian school, it all made apocalypse seem so real, so tangible, so close that i was constantly haunted by dreams and worries about what would happen if i found out who the antichrist was.&quot;<br /><br />On pg. 22 He says, It confirmed what i had already known from the beginning--that i wouldn't be saved like everybody else. I knew it everyday when i left for school trembling with fear that the world would end, I wouldn't go to Heaven and i'd never seee my parents again. But after a year had passed, then another, then another, and the world and Mrs. Price (teacher) and Brian Warner adn the prostitutes who were born again were still there, i felt cheated and lied to. Gradually, i began to resent christian school and doubt everything I was told. It became clear that the suffering that they were praying to be released from was a suffering that they had imposed on themselves--and now us. the beast they lived in fear of was really themselves: It was man, not some mythological demon, that was going to destroy man in the end. And this beast had been created out of their fear. THE SEEDS OF WHO I AM NOW HAD BEEN PLANTED.&quot;<br /><br />He goes on to relate how he got heavy into rock, especially heavy metal and also dungeons and dragons which were forbidden fruit of the christian school which further fed his anarchy.<br /><br />On pg 31, he says, &quot;christian school had prepared me well for public school. It defined the taboos, then held then away at arms length, leaving me reaching for them in vain. As soon as i switched schools, it was all there for the taking---sex, drugs, rock, the occult. I didn't even have to look for them: they found me.&quot;<br /><br />Early in highschool, he got into black magic and stated on pg. 45 that he &quot;could use black magic to turn the lowly lot life had given me around--to attain a position of power that other people would envy and accomplish things that other people couldn't.&quot;<br /><br />His reasoning for calling himself manson are as follows on pg. 79, &quot; Manson was the perfect story protagonist for a frustrated writer like myself. He was a character who, because of his contempt for the world around him and, more so, himself, does everything he can to trick people into liking him. And then, once he wins their confidence, he uses it to destroy them.<br /><br />On pg. 213 he says, &quot;When my dreams about the antichrist began occuring more frequently later in life, i knew i was that figure.&quot;<br /><br />On pg. 219 he says, &quot; I can't even stand to watch people in restaurants laughing, having fun, enjoying life. Their pitiful happiness sickens me...I'd love to kill all of them, but i'd be doing them a favor. The worst punishment i can give them is to let them wake up every morning and lead their stupid f...g lives, let them raise their stupid f...g children and of course, make a record call AntiChrist Superstar, which will annoy and destroy each and every one of them. F... you America. The world spreads its legs for another f...g star...&quot; (amazon denied this review due to the f words so I had to delete them. Ironically, they will sell the book with profanity, but not allow the words to be quoted.)<br /><br />So in a nutshell, these quotes helped me to realize that Marilyn Manson's music is not for merely entertaining people. There is truly method behind his madness. He has a message for the world to hear. He is a prophet of doom and hatred. Although he has a point about americans and their blind worship for superstars, his contempt for humanity and life is blatant. 1Peter 5:8 says that Satan is as a roaring lion going about seeking those he may devour, this mindset sounds very much in harmony with the mindset of marilyn manson. Marilyn Mason is on an egotistical trip seeking for all the world to worship him.ing, having fun, enjoying life. B006UG90RM|4.0|I am stunned.  I really did not expect this record to be this good.  Like most VH fans, I thought they had nothing left in the tank.  Certainly the Sammy Hagar years did nothing to dissuade me of that notion.  I bought \\"5150,\\" hated it and never touched another Van Halen record until this one.  For Van Halen to really work, it has to have the two things Sammy Hagar does not possess:  personality and a sense of humor.  Putting a 6th grade-level pun into an album title is a sign that something is wrong.  OU812?  For Unlawful Carnal Knowledge?  That is just plain embarrassing.  At least David Lee Roth has style.  You may not like it, but he has it.  Sammy Hagar was and is as bland as an unsalted Saltine cracker.  So Roth returning to the group gave me a little hope.  This album is incredible.  A lot of people are going to slam it for being full of reworked demo tracks from before the first Van Halen album.  This is true, but if you listen to those same early demos, you would hear a LOT of bits and pieces of what would become \\"Mean Streets,\\" \\"Hang 'Em High,\\" \\"Where Have All the Good Times Gone\\" (solo bit,) \\"Hot For Teacher\\" and many many others.  So it isn't like Van Halen never cribbed from their own early material before.  On this record, Eddie Van Halen is on top of his game. Losing a third of his tongue, having a hip replaced, battling cancer and addiction...none of these things seems to have done anything but improve his playing.  It is as though getting out from under the syrupy ballad schmaltz of Sammy Hagar has lit the fire back in him.  Or maybe it is the infusion of youth, having Wolf Van Halen playing the bass.  Perhaps Wolfie has inspired his aging guitar master father to regain the fire of his own youth.  As for Wolf, his playing is solid, at times impressive.  If Eddie did not play the bass lines on the record, then I am indeed impressed with Wolf's playing.  His backing vocals have easily taken up the slack for the missing Michael Anthony as well.  Alex Van Halen is still banging the crap out of his drum kit.  He is still as energetic and frenetic as ever, providing a rock solid hard rock foundation for this band.  David Lee Roth is back in form as well.  Gone are the screams that punctuated VH songs in the past, too often, if you think about it.  He will let loose with one here and there.  But he has increased his range a bit over the years.  And he was never that much about the singing, as much as being about the showmanship and the fronting of the band.  All of that is back.  His sense of humor and playfulness is back as well.  And perhaps most important of all, there isn't a \\"Love Walks In\\" or \\"When Its Love\\" type song anywhere on this record.  Such crap was never a strong suit of Van Halen.  For Van Halen fans, TRUE Van Halen fans, the ones who were disgusted and disheartened at the malaise of the Van Hagar years, this is a welcome return to blistering hard rock and roll.  Thank God.  There is far too little of it these days.

030788743X|3.0|A light, fun romp through a well-imagined, if largely plagiarised, near-future universe. It's hard not to enjoy these sort of monomyth hero-quest stories. I started out hating the book but by then end I was eager to find out what happened next. A page-turner for sure.  That said, it has some serious issues. I didn't find the constant 80s references as annoying as some reviewers as that's the central conceit of the book, so what do you expect? But the prose is of really poor quality. As other reviewers have pointed out, it's barely even young-adult level. Usually very simple description of the \\"I went here and did this, then I went there and did that\\" variety. Mountains of cliche. And if you want a good drinking game, try having one every time Cline has the first person narrator append the adverb \\"quickly\\" to some action he's carrying out. You'll be blackout drunk by about Chapter 4.  There's also reams of naked exposition. Apparently the admonition \\"Show, don't tell\\" is something Cline has never heard. I listened to the audiobook version and the first two hours or more was virtually all pure information, as if recited from an encyclopedia. I felt like I was being read a summary of the book, rather than the book itself.  The main character is a bit of a Mary Sue and the supporting characters are all off the shelf archetypes, but it doesn't really matter in a story of this type, although the dialogue is painfully bad in places. It's all about the plot, which is engaging and paced well.  This was a piece of fluff book along the lines of The Da Vinci Code, but for nerds. It has the potential to be a good movie, where Cline will be forced to show and not tell and the bad prose won't matter. They'll probably need a script doctor to fix the dialogue though.  If you want to read a book along these lines with better writing and more interesting ideas, I recommend Neal Stephenson's Snow Crash. Despite the latter's pacing and structure issues, it is a much better book than this. Hopefully the movie will live up to its potential.

0439023521|3.0|On the surface, `The Hunger Games' is an intense read, diving headfirst into a possible future where a tyrant government controls a country's food supply/wealth/and even lives.  There once was 13 separate districts.  You can not leave your designated district.  If you do, you will either face a horrid future, you have to watch 24-hours worth of Twilight - or worse - death  - though I suspect watching Twilight for 24-hours straight is equal to death!  One district ended up rebelling, and well - there's now 12 districts.  Because the tyrant government wants to prove that they are the worst government since the W. Bush days, they decide to create a gruesome televised game called `The Hunger Games' - two kids, 12 to 16 years old from each district, is drafted into this game.  The story is narrated by a young teen that volunteer after hearing her younger sister drafted.  From there on, the story, told by her, shifts to the preparation of the game, and finally, the game itself.  A bunch of kids are placed in a jungle-type-area where they have to hunt for themselves, feed for themselves, and kill each other until there is one declared winner.  And the game has many twists and turns, enticing both the viewer of the games being aired in the story, and the reader.  For a young adult book, it's quite intense (though the death scenes are obviously toned down a bit).  I can understand some of its appeal.  And I can sense as I was reading the book, the subtle references to where the author proclaimed she got inspired to write this story: the Iraq war (which had been televised heavily), and the reality TV fad, which plagues every single TV station and network.  It does make you question the unnecessary shock-value reality TV does, and its superficial viewers that continue to thrive on the reality TV hell.  Will it ever come to this: watching young kid's murder each other for the good of entertainment?  I mean, we already have a show about teeny-boppers being knocked up on MTV.  What's next?  It's good to know, though, that networks do have some limits.  NBC just recently pulled a Fear Factor episode where one lady drinks a large glass of donkey urine, and another lady drinks a large glass of donkey semen.  America, the proud?  For anyone that's under the age of 20, this book will probably appeal to you.  It's very well written, presented nicely, and almost makes you believe this book is a real memoir.  But for someone (like me) that is quite familiar with the sci-fi genre, there isn't one single moment in this book that is original.  Predictable and even a bit clich (especially the love triangle - UGH!), I expected more with all the hype.  Even those shock-and-awe moments through out the `game' were predictable.  The broadcasting of death on TV for entertainment value has been, dare I say, done to death.  With that said, I think of this book as what it should be - a nicely told homage to the sci-fi genre.  Suzanne Collins did not reinvent the genre, and it certainly doesn't add anything new.  But for what it is worth, it kept me entertained.  I can understand the appeal under-20 age group.  I don't understand the hype over 20.  Come on people - read Stephen King's `The Bachman Books!'  I am interested in the movie, but I am not interested in the remaining `trilogy.'  I pretty much know where it is going...which saddens me because I really wanted to read something imaginative, instead of recycled.

0545259088|4.0|*This review is for ALL THREE book in this series.*  Age Group: solid YA (implied/described sexual content; graphic violence)  Rating: Idea 4; Execution 4; Style 4.5  Genre: romantic fantasy  Shiver / Linger  Grace is relatively happy living in her rural home with parents who are rarely ever there...it's lonely, but she creates her own structure: good grades, no late nights...  Only when she begins to feel drawn away from the warmth and light into the quick chill of the Boundary Wood that fringes her house--toward the pack of wolves that roams there--does she realize that something is missing, and perhaps always has been.  Then the boy appears--and he is one of them, she is sure. His yellow eyes belong to the wolf, the stormy gray wolf who saved her all those years ago.  And then, he is Sam. And she cannot imagine life without him. So she doesn't...  ...but the cold, and his earthy, shaggy, winter body remembers. It will claim him.  It might be calling her, too.  Forever  Sam is stable...as much as he can be without Grace.  Where she is...what she is, he can't be sure. He only knows he needs to find her, fast.  Now that a second death has been blamed on his pack, they only have so much time before the hunters begin closing in. It may already be too late.  ____________________________________________________________  This series is sort of in a class by itself. It is a dark, brooding romantic fantasy of a similar brand as that of Twilight, but the style (and LACK of love triangle) is superior enough to make that comparison invalid. And, I think the quality of the writing has only improved with age. Out now, the third book has all of the same qualities as Shiver and Linger, only more crystallized and focused.  For one, the world is built with integrity. Fitting to the plot, it is tight and limited, but the text makes it visceral. Metaphor is a key descriptive player and it is used in fresh and surprising ways. Sam's favorite poet Maria Rainer Rilke is quoted often,and I daresay the text benefits immensely from that--in style as much as in content.  The characters are multi-dimensional, but focused and consistent. They grown and learn--about themselves and others--over the course of the trilogy. (You would think that would be a given, but I've learned the hard way that this is an unusual characteristic in YA lit these days! So, when I see it, I like to point it out.) The side characters are intriguing (bad-boy Cole is there for those of us who like a scruffy egomaniac) and take on a life of their own as they become more central to the storyline in Forever. This is in part due to the multiple-point-of-view format of the book, which I found refreshing (each chapter is written in a different character's voice). Overall, they (Sam, Grace, Cole, Isabel, etc) aren't extremely deep, and sometimes their realizations are slightly obvious, but it's not to the point of annoying. The crux of the book really is the plight of the werewolf, and, of course, the two soul mates caught in the crossfire between an errant biology and an ignorant humanity.  Yes, it's a tender, emo love story. However, even though soft, skinny, sensitive Sam is not really my type, he is such a well-drawn character, you can't help but like him. The poetry--Rilke's, not necessarily Sam's--often would step in to redeem him for me, giving him a deeper facet than most other series' boy toys have. He so clearly needs Grace that you can't help but root for them. Grace herself is all but perfect, but not in an unlikable way. She's beautiful, but strong (kind of like Bella Swan should have been).  Really, for all of the wolf in them, they are both very human.  The plot is fairly strong: catching and medium-paced. I got slightly impatient at times, so it wouldn't have worked for me to read all 3 books in a row. I read them as they came out, and I was actually happy with that. The world is compelling enough to make revisiting a pleasure. Some reviewers did not like the endingof the trilogy, but I did. True, it's ambivalent, but not in a cheap, incongruent, copout, \\"no questions answered\\" way like the Incarceron series. There's the hint of what could happen, but not the confirmation. Frankly, it totally fits with the style, and I was ok with that.  In fact, the most striking characteristic of the books for me lies in the style--in the \\"feeling\\" the writing gives: of cold.  True, the story is purposefully set on the borders of a wilderness in Mercy Falls, Minnesota, where winters are long and harsh: cold is what makes a werewolf shift into a wolf. But it's one thing to simply state that fact--another to weave it into the fabric of the text. I don't quite know how to explain what I mean, but for all the warmth of Sam and Grace's relationship, I always came away from the text with a chill. It was as if there was somehow something delicate lying between me and them, a subtle frosty whisper that fills and parts the air.  That, I found totally masterful.  [Review originally published at Booknotized.com.]

0312641893|4.0|Age Group: YA (totally clean)  Genre: Scifi + Post-Apocalyptic  Rating Breakdown: Idea 5+; Execution 3+  Cinder is a cyborg--which in her society means barely human. Somewhere in a past she can't remember, there was an accident that left her parentless and a surgery that gave her a foot and a hand she doesn't want, but can't live without. Her one glimmer of hope came in the form of a scientist from the Eastern commonwealth--a conglomerate of countries united by the emperor sometime after the devastating WW4--but was dashed before she even know about him, when he died of the Letumosis epidemic.  Now, stuck with a stepmother who hates her, working as a mechanic to pay bills that aren't her own, in a country swept by plague and threatened by war from the psychotic Lunar queen, Cinder only dreams of escape.  And, then the Prince walks in.  __________________________________________________________________________________________________  First off, you must read this book. It's great. You'll enjoy it, I guarantee. It's overall tightly written, fast paced, and unique. I was drawn to the cover at first sight and couldn't put it down!  But, as a connoisseur (hah!) I am truly on the fence about it. As a reviewer, I feel compelled to tell the whole truth and nothing but. So, while on the one hand it was a thrilling read, it has its flaws.  I love the concept--very original. Instead of a technologically backwards future of the sort we're used to seeing in today's post-apocalyptic or dystopian novels, this one is very techno-savvy. Reminded me of Westerfeld's The Uglies in its tenor. Hover cars are all the rage, and human beings survive historically fatal or utterly disfiguring injuries because of extreme advances in surgical prosthetics.  Fairly logically-advanced versions of the internet, computers, and televisions are the main form of communication and entertainment, which helps tie nicely into the present-day world.  The commonwealth, and its emperor are situated in Asia, in a city called New Beijing, making this the second book to allude to fears of China's rise to world power. However, unlike some, this book doesn't see that as a bad thing. The Emperor and his family are just, fair rulers, and peacefully cohabitate with other ruling bodies--except those on the moon (yup!).  Instead of climate change, and/or oil shortage being the cause of the apocalypse (both of which have been almost overdone these days...), it's international conflict that spawned both destruction and peace between Earthen countries. Unchecked greed and power hunger is the reason for the latest threat from the mutated inhabitants of the moon--and their mind-controlling queen.  Up until that point, everything was great. But, the mind control was where the book started to get under my skin. I found it to be too convenient, cheesy, inconsistently implemented, and ineffective.  Similarly, though I appreciated the attempt to align this with the original Cinderella story, a few of the plot elements at the end were too forced. This is a problem I have with a lot of fairytale re-imaginings: they don't seem to realize you can pick and choose which elements to include--and even then, you can bend them a little to fit your setting. This book tried too hard to get them all in...and didn't bend or wield them skillfully enough to fit. For one, the \\"carriage\\" (an old rusted out automobile) was a potentially really intriguing twist that just didn't deliver and faded out as abruptly as it appeared. More importantly, however, the ball was just totally out of place, in my mind. Not only was it a virtual impossibility (all the citizens of one major city invited at once?), but then, why such an outdated ritual in full 17th century style was the one tradition that held over from the old to such a futuristic world wasn't explained. It wasn't the right scene for the confrontation, and as a result, the final scenes were just not cathartic enough.  As for characters, the evil queen wasn't very believable--her dialogue made me chuckle at some points. And, last but not least, Cinder just wasn't a decisive or confident enough character for my taste. Why she waited so long to work on the Prince's robot--meanwhile the dropped hints and reminders of its import were abundant--I just couldn't figure out. And her two obsessions--her own imperfection and escape--were overdone to the point of threatening the story not only plot-wise, but overall by making her slightly annoying.  That said, however, I really enjoyed the concept and the world, and will definitely be looking out for book 2--which I hope will benefit from its freedom from the original story and license to do fantastic things with a fantastic concept.  [Review originally published at Booknotized.com.]

0545123291|4.0|*This review is for ALL THREE book in this series.*<br /><br />Age Group: solid YA (implied/described sexual content; graphic violence)<br />Rating: Idea 4'; Execution 4'; Style 4.5'<br />Genre: romantic fantasy<br /><br />Shiver / Linger<br /><br />Grace is relatively happy living in her rural home with parents who are rarely ever there...it's lonely, but she creates her own structure: good grades, no late nights...<br />Only when she begins to feel drawn away from the warmth and light into the quick chill of the Boundary Wood that fringes her house--toward the pack of wolves that roams there--does she realize that something is missing, and perhaps always has been.<br />Then the boy appears--and he is one of them, she is sure. His yellow eyes belong to the wolf, the stormy gray wolf who saved her all those years ago.<br />And then, he is Sam. And she cannot imagine life without him. So she doesn't...<br />...but the cold, and his earthy, shaggy, winter body remembers. It will claim him.<br />It might be calling her, too.<br /><br />Forever<br /><br />Sam is stable...as much as he can be without Grace.<br />Where she is...what she is, he can't be sure. He only knows he needs to find her, fast.<br />Now that a second death has been blamed on his pack, they only have so much time before the hunters begin closing in. It may already be too late.<br /><br />____________________________________________________________<br /><br />This series is sort of in a class by itself. It is a dark, brooding romantic fantasy of a similar brand as that of Twilight, but the style (and LACK of love triangle) is superior enough to make that comparison invalid. And, I think the quality of the writing has only improved with age. Out now, the third book has all of the same qualities as Shiver and Linger, only more crystallized and focused.<br /><br />For one, the world is built with integrity. Fitting to the plot, it is tight and limited, but the text makes it visceral. Metaphor is a key descriptive player and it is used in fresh and surprising ways. Sam's favorite poet Maria Rainer Rilke is quoted often, and I daresay the text benefits immensely from that--in style as much as in content.<br /><br />The characters are multi-dimensional, but focused and consistent. They grown and learn--about themselves and others--over the course of the trilogy. (You would think that would be a given, but I've learned the hard way that this is an unusual characteristic in YA lit these days! So, when I see it, I like to point it out.) The side characters are intriguing (bad-boy Cole is there for those of us who like a scruffy egomaniac) and take on a life of their own as they become more central to the storyline in Forever. This is in part due to the multiple-point-of-view format of the book, which I found refreshing (each chapter is written in a different character's voice). Overall, they (Sam, Grace, Cole, Isabel, etc) aren't extremely deep, and sometimes their realizations are slightly obvious, but it's not to the point of annoying. The crux of the book really is the plight of the werewolf, and, of course, the two soul mates caught in the crossfire between an errant biology and an ignorant humanity.<br /><br />Yes, it's a tender, emo love story. However, even though soft, skinny, sensitive Sam is not really my type, he is such a well-drawn character, you can't help but like him. The poetry--Rilke's, not necessarily Sam's--often would step in to redeem him for me, giving him a deeper facet than most other series' boy toys have. He so clearly needs Grace that you can't help but root for them. Grace herself is all but perfect, but not in an unlikable way. She's beautiful, but strong (kind of like Bella Swan should have been).<br /><br />Really, for all of the wolf in them, they are both very human.<br /><br />The plot is fairly strong: catching and medium-paced. I got slightly impatient at times, so it wouldn't have worked for me to read all 3 books in a row. I read them as they came out, and I was actually happy with that. The world is compelling enough to make revisiting a pleasure. Some reviewers did not like the ending of the trilogy, but I did. True, it's ambivalent, but not in a cheap, incongruent, copout, \\"no questions answered\\" way like the Incarceron series. There's the hint of what could happen, but not the confirmation. Frankly, it totally fits with the style, and I was ok with that.<br /><br />In fact, the most striking characteristic of the books for me lies in the style--in the \\"feeling\\" the writing gives: of cold.<br /><br />True, the story is purposefully set on the borders of a wilderness in Mercy Falls, Minnesota, where winters are long and harsh: cold is what makes a werewolf shift into a wolf. But it's one thing to simply state that fact--another to weave it into the fabric of the text. I don't quite know how to explain what I mean, but for all the warmth of Sam and Grace's relationship, I always came away from the text with a chill. It was as if there was somehow something delicate lying between me and them, a subtle frosty whisper that fills and parts the air.<br /><br />That, I found totally masterful.<br /><br />[Review originally published at Booknotized.com.]of the trilogy, but I did. B006UG90RM|5.0|I did a quick review the second I heard the whole album (which I thought was amazing). Now, after reading everyone's reviews and listening to the record about 30 times through, I wanted to write a more educated review and respond to a few strange rumors that seem to have taken hold of the reviewer-base, here, on Amazon and elsewhere.  1. LET'S TALK ABOUT DEMOS VS. NEW MATERIAL  First off, this album is aggressive and hearkens back to everything we all loved about VH in the 70's and 80's. At some point, it was noticed that there is some re-hash of early Warner Brothers Demos and lost/under-heard tracks. That statement somehow morphed into \\"All these tunes are old demos.\\" THIS IS NOT TRUE. Over the weekend, I listened to all the old demos and lost tracks and B-sides (about 50 of them or so) and only about a third of this record is culled from old stuff. The rest is brand-spankin' new. Also, the songs that are from old material are so heavily re-worked that they're almost unrecognizable from their original versions from the 70's. Those original demos are so raw and unrefined that they are almost a joke to listen to at times. Now, those tracks that they did go back to are crisp and fun and gritty and nicely finished. They are now cohesive and ballsy and they KILL. I am a musician who knows a lot of musicians. EVERYBODY goes back to the well for every album. Whether or not they lie about it is another story. This is how it is. Van Halen is no different. Why not go back to the great leftovers? Why waste them? They are good tunes.  It should be noted that Van Halen has always culled material from the old demo days when Eddie was a riff machine. All the way through the Sammy Hagar years (Van Hagar as fans like to call it) they were doing this. As a matter of fact, the first three David Lee Roth albums (and even some of the fourth) almost come entirely from these 1975/1976 demos (read Sammy's Autobiography for more on this fact). Also, the fans have clamored to have studio recordings of these unreleased tracks for years. Now that we finally get what we've been asking for, we're going to criticize it? C'mon, guys... let's not be hypocritical. This is what we asked for. This is what we got and IT ROCKS... HARD!!!  Here's a short breakdown of which songs come from where: \\"Big River\\" is taken from a demo called \\"Big Trouble.\\" \\"Beats Workin'\\" is taken from a demo called \\"Put Out the Lights.\\" \\"Blood and Fire's\\" instrumental bits are taken from a soundtrack song from the film \\"The Wild Life.\\" The track is called \\"Donut City.\\" The major riff from \\"Outta Space\\" is taken from a demo called \\"Let's get Rockin'.\\" \\"Riffs from \\"Bullethead\\" seem familiar from those demos as do a few of the solo riffs throughout the new record, but nothing specific and \\"She's the Woman\\" is almost a direct demo from the original of the same name. That's pretty much it. Really.  2. LET'S TALK ABOUT DAVE'S VOICE  Some of the negative reviews of David Lee Roth's vocals on this record astound me. They say he sounds old and just doesn't have it anymore. Guess what, guys? HE IS OLD! He's old and he still sounds just as good as he did around the time of \\"SKYSCRAPER.\\" As a matter of fact, he could never REALLY sing and we all loved him for it. It was his energy and fun-loving lyrics that we dug. All of that is right here on this album. By the time \\"1984\\" rolled in, he was already straining a bit on the highs and doing the deep-voiced rap thing (see \\"Panama\\"). We didn't seem to care then. When \\"Crazy from the Heat\\" and \\"Eat Em and Smile\\" came around, this became part of his singing style. We all bought those records, too.  All that said, does DLR sound like he did in 1977? No, of course not... and neither do you or I. He still gives us that energy and fun old-school feel that we love.  3. THE PRODUCTION/SOUND  All of the producers and engineers involved in this record clearly had a notion of what Van Halen should sound like. There is a raw/live quality to these tracks which notonly makes them sound gritty, fun and excellent, but also makes them sit nicely alongside the original 6 records. There is a bit of a \\"wall of sound\\" thing happening here and there which can sound a touch muddy, but it also creates an awesome energy that almost makes one feel as though they are experiencing the songs in a live performance. Personally, I dig it.  4. OVERALL  I am honestly astounded with how great this album is, overall. I just expected a fun album that I would probably have to make excuses for to myself and convince myself that I liked, but... Wow No excuses needed. The fun is here, the drive and meat are here, the singable qualities are here, the raw grit is here... This is rock.  Dave... You say thanks for the dream in the liner notes. No, man... Thank YOU for the dream. You made me feel like a kid again. A feat not to be underestimated.  Well done VH

Filmed live at the glorious Royal Albert Hall for a special three-performance 25th Anniversary event in October 2011, Andrew Lloyd Webber's THE PHANTOM OF THE OPERA sparkles like never before, with 100 cast members, and a 100-piece orchestra.<br /><br />I now completely understand why Lloyd Webber personally selected Sierra Boggess and Ramin  Karimloo (the original London leads of his sequel \\"Love Never Dies\\", which closed a few weeks before the 25th Anniversary of PHANTOM) to once again play Christine and the Phantom.  Their chemistry is like nothing else I've ever witnessed.  And only by playing opposite each other for quite some time can a set of Christine/Phantom's ever hope to match it.  The stage literally quivers when the pair first comes together, and by the time they've performed their erotic Act 2 duet \\"The Point of No Return\\", let's just say that fireworks wouldn't do them justice.  Ms Boggess will particularly astound you with her incredible acting performance as Christine - very rarely have I encountered such a full-blooded Christine in the many times I've seen the show onstage.<br /><br />There are naturally some shortcomings and restrictions in performing the full PHANTOM in a concert hall space.  Gone are some of the more spectacular effects (the famous chandelier no longer rises or falls but \\"explodes\\" at the climax of Act 1 with the aid of some pyrotechnic magic).  LED background projections provide the various scene changes; however the entire thing is closely modelled after the original Maria Bjornson designs - right down to the golden  proscenium arch.  PHANTOM purists will appreciate the lengths that have been taken to present the show in its signature look.<br /><br />The rest of the principals have all been associated with their PHANTOM roles in the past, with the exception being Hadley Fraser as Raoul.  Fraser's debut as Raoul is amongst the finest I've witnessed, taking who can be a rather prissy one-dimensional hero to a man who is a more than worthy adversary for the Phantom.<br /><br />Liz Robertson is Madame Giry (in addition to the London production she has also played Giry in \\"Love Never Dies\\"), and the opera house managers are played by Barry James and Gareth Snook.  Wendy Ferguson (one of the best Carlotta's from PHANTOM's London run) came in at the last minute to replace an actual opera singer who had been cast as Carlotta, but who had fallen ill.  Ferguson's Carlotta is a much more sympathetic character than what is usually played, and she is truly deserving of having her performance finally documented here.  Wynne Evans plays Piangi, whilst international ballet star Sergei Polunin dances the Slave Master in \\"Hannibal\\" as well as the lead role in the \\"Il Muto\\" ballet.<br /><br />More surprises are revealed during the \\"Grand Finale\\" as Andrew Lloyd Webber brings Michael Crawford, the surviving original London cast members and the creative team onstage for a well-deserved ovation. B00005ASHM|5.0|In this modern day and age its easy to pinpoint the relevance of music albums because of their place in history. However,what's truly difficult is to consider those albums to sound as fresh and innovative as they were at the time of their release. Pet Sounds, however, makes it seem like the easiest task in the world. This is the case because, superficially, Pet Sounds is the first and main point of reference for the wide scope of innovations to come out of 60s' pop. It seems everything the 60s' had to offer to pop music were synthesized within the album; from the innovations of earlier craftsmen like Phil Spector and Burt Bacharach, the growth of the Baroque Pop aesthetic, and the California sunshine scene, all the way to the upcoming of Sgt. Pepper, late 60s' American psychedelia, and the Chamber/Twee Pop revival in the 1990s' (to which this album was heralded as a rosetta stone).  Sure, to must pundits this is all very possible to point out without even listening to the album. But once you get to listen to it, with the first listen it sounds as nothing The Beach Boys had ever done (or would do again). To be blunt, the album easily trashes out the Boys' entire previous catalogue, making it seem as if nothing was even listenable before Pet Sounds. This is of course not the case, the point being that the album is at such an elevated high from the (already good) albums of the rest of their catalogue.  Thats also not to say that this is a different style than their previous albums, its just that every element of the Boys' music was not perfected up until Pet Sounds. Their classic vocal harmonies are present; but Wilson multi-layers them into angelical synchronization. The pop hooks are still there; but Wilson adorns them with subtle juxtapositions of varied orchestrations (within the same song). The teenage melodramatic mood? Well in Pet Sounds it is transformed into timeless yearns for love, reciprocation, and meaning. See the pattern that emerges?  The one thing thats truly radical from the Boys' previous inputs, and arguably what gives Pet Sounds its sublimity, is the instrumentation. No longer are the Boys' reliant on just blues chord progression piano, surf-like guitar hooks and saxophone here and there. This time, Wilson is able to integrate the grandeur of his best songs (like California Girls and Don't Worry Baby) into a full fledged album. And not only that, he uses an full set orchestra to conceive it. This is not just your typical strings and brass however; Wilson goes all the way with timpani, french horns, vibraphone and harpsichord, among others. He even goes beyond orchestral instruments and brings in ukuleles, hammond organs, hawaiian percussion, accordion, hell you name it! Its just like one critic pointed out, the singles from Pet Sounds where the only time the Boys' met commercial success while being endlessly experimental.  The songs are as varied as the instrumentation as well. You have the sunshine-like opening of \\"Wouldn't It Be Nice\\", the hauntingly sad \\"Don't Talk\\", the superficial fun of \\"Sloop John B.\\" and the anthemic \\"God Only Knows\\", just to mention a few examples. Hell, there are even a couple of instrumental tracks a-la Martin Denny! Be certain of one thing, there are NO high points and low points in this album, it all flows majestically like vintage wine from beginning to end.  Even though Pet Sounds is a mono recording (stereo listeners don't stop reading YET), Brian Wilson unmistakably manages the production output into an elaborate, wall-of-sound-alike work. That is to say, it is essentially mono, but the recording process of the orchestrations are in no way lessened. To be honest, I thought at first that it was a stereo recording, because I can notice the difference with some of the Boys' previous records, in which the instrumentation could sometimes be unbearably washed up. And its also a bona-fide experience to listen to it in mono, as Brian intended it to be. However if you're really into stereo, there is a stereo version of the album included (past the bonus track \\"Hang On To Your Ego\\").  To conclude, I believe that if you're going to buy just one 60s' pop album in your life then let Pet Sounds be that choice. I mean sure, you could pick up one of their much rivaled Beatles albums, but not even Sgt. Pepper matches the ornamental beauty of Pet Sounds, The Beach Boys one true magnum opus...although I am yet to listen to The Smile Sessions, so who knows if there's a contender...

B000002KM6|5.0|Since the 70's drew to a close,there'd been increasing tensions between Eddie Van Halen and David Lee Roth over which element of the bands sound would triumph;the vocal and performance theatrics of Diamond Dave or Eddie's musicianship. Truth be told,both were equally important. But hey. Look at the Byrds,The Kinks and The Sex Pistols. These bands seemed to thrive creatively in states of near constant bickering and rivalry-challenging the other party they dealt with to do just a little bit better. So they battle raged on this fourth Van Halen set. And what are they doing to advance their the whole project? The pair along with the other two members of the band are kind of challenging each other internally with new and different musical ideas very atypical of Van Halen thus far,seeing how they could make it work for themselves and their audience. In the end this album wasn't a big commercial dynamo at all.  But the end result is probably,to my hears one of the most musically advanced and penetrating album I've heard them do. And likely one of my very favorites.<br /><br />  Never shying from a good groove at any time,one thing about this album is it rocks yes. But it doesn't rock as hard or noisily as some of their earlier ones did. It's not as greasy or spiky. The rockier songs \\"Dirty Movies\\",\\"Sinner's Swing\\",\\"Unchained\\" and \\"Hear About It Later\\" don't really seem to be that dominant of the album. And most important they rely more on texture and bassy sounds than rocking upfront guitar solos. One thing I really like about this album is it's open and proud emphasis on funkiness. \\"Mean Street\\" comes out of this rocking,slower groove and is probably the funkiest Van Halen album opener. \\"Push Comes To Show\\" is not only my favorite song here,and funky but sophistifunk at that-with heavy layered keyboard sounds and a more arranged production within the groove. \\"So Is The Love?\\" has a soulful boogie rock flavor to it. And there's some out and out experimental,almost electronic/new wave type flavors on \\"Sunday Afternoon In The Part\\" before the faster guitar rock does return on \\"One Foot Out The Door\\"<br /><br />  On an album matching themes of confrontation and attitude with music that doesn't always sound like anything Van Halen had yet done up to this point,you can really understand how significant it was that the members of the band chose a funk groove as an instrumental mediation tool as it were to try to solve their conflicts. For one,it's not a rhythmic pattern that tends to emphasize much \\"guitar hero\\" type antics. Even a lead guitarist soloist in that music is generally forced to keep it clean somehow and melt into the rhythmic complexities of the musical whole. And even on the harder rockers here,there is this interesting sense of unity among the players. Especially considering the two titanic egos of the band were on an emotional collision course during these sessions. It's solid proof that if handled right even rock and roll,a musical genre often revolved around the most decedent of megalomaniacs,can weather some difficult personal problems if they can find a way to resolve it through new and challenging musical idioms. While few people really absorbed it that heavily in it's time,it's for sure one of the bands most purely creative triumphs and showed they were a lot more than your simple proto hair metal arena band.e type flavors on \\"Sunday Afternoon In The Part\\" before the faster guitar rock does return on \\"One Foot Out The Door\\"    On an album matching themes of confrontation and attitude with music that doesn't always sound like anything Van Halen had yet done up to this point,you can really understand how significant it was that the members of the band chose a funk groove as an instrumental mediation tool as it were to try to solve their conflicts. B00004Y6OA|4.0|Well the 1980's had arrived. And Van Halen had two big smash hit albums in a row with their debut [[ASIN:B00004Y6O9 Van Halen]] and the follow up [[ASIN:B00004Y6O8 Van Halen II]],which are both ideal additions to the collection of any rock and roll fan. They had enough charisma and instrumental talent to fill rock clubs and stadiums of all sorts. But they weren't idiots either. They knew they they couldn't just clone a good thing again and again just to keep their relationship stable with the public. They had to take some calculated risks. This era wasn't a good time for that. There was a crossroads going on. And as with many people Van Halen needed to contend as to how far they'd get caught up in the tide. So here they decided to dig as deep as they can into their type of rock music. The band on the cover are depicted in a suggestive huddling \\"hump\\" of Van Halen's axe. And the inside photo of Diamond Dave whering nothing but those ultra tight black leather pants of his alone with his hands literally chained? Well that photography says more about what's hear then you might think.<br /><br />  Where one might view Roth's lustiness on their first two albums as being almost playful in a masculine adult sort of way,the theme of this album is sexual aggression and what leads up to it. They start right off with the more catchy hit song \\"And The Cradle Will Rock\\" pretty much right off. I am not sure if this album was quite the success of the first two commercially. Aside from that this is still a very clean and sleekly produced album. But much louder and faster rock overall. On songs like \\"Fools\\",\\"Tora! Tora!\\" and \\"Loss Of Control\\" Roth and Eddie are both lyrically and musically exploring the impulsive sexual jungle attitude that most rock stars embrace on a surface level. But it goes a little deeper into the motivations of ones physical desires. Diamond Dave sings it,Eddie plays it,with some of his solos downright masturbatory. The blues comes into it on the main diversion from the hard rock on \\"Take Your Whiskey Home\\",interestingly enough the albums source of wit and reflection.<br /><br />  While I'd hardly describe this album as overly intellectual,it's not exactly possessed of the same kind of reckless abandon that the first two records possessed. These are people and musicians looking more into the face of maturity. And how to do what they do as they continue to age. It's only appropriate that,considering even as tough rocking as this album is that it runs so deeply into the blues. These songs make no apologies,they never complain. They just kind of let you know that some of these guys are sexually frustrated,are in need of an outlet and maybe some past repression might have a part to play it in. I don't think anyone intended this to be about the door closing on the sexual revolution. But somehow it's there. And it's only appropriate this album is somewhat darker,if not cynical and negative. It isn't grunge,goth or mope rock or anything like that. It just deals with the rock n roll attitude with a little more reflection. And allows Eddie to continue expanding his soloing style along the way. Not one of the bands better known albums. But very important to their future development.hard rock on \\"Take Your Whiskey Home\\",interestingly enough the albums source of wit and reflection. B0052U59F4|4.0|\\"The only man I've ever been attracted to, and he comes with a bloody contract, a flogger and a whole world of issues.\\"<br /><br />That quote just about sums up the problems that heroine Anastasia Steele is up against in FIFTY SHADES OF GREY. Ana is a sexually innocent 21-year-old on the verge of college graduation when she meets drop-dead sexy, 27-year-old multimillionaire business tycoon, Christian Grey. Christian becomes immediately fascinated by Ana, and begins to pursue her, even though he continuously warns that he's all wrong for her. As Ana succumbs to the powerhouse that is Christian Grey, it doesn't take her long to realize that no man, no matter how unbelievably hot he is, is perfect--and Christian is far from it.<br /><br />While Christian is a dominant in the business world, he's actually a \\"Dom\\" in the bedroom, and proposes a contract based D/s relationship with Ana. Although Christian has had a string of these relationships (15 to be exact) before meeting Ana, he begins to make it clear to her through many avenues that she affects him in a new and different way than any other woman he has ever been involved with. Over the course of Fifty Shades of Grey, Ana begins to chip away at the hard, complicated outer shell of Christian, forcing him to admit things he's never told anyone, and causing him to break many of his own rules in the process.<br /><br />Having had an incredibly disturbing and traumatizing start to life before he was adopted at the age of four, a subject he only barely broaches with Ana, Christian carries an unbelievable amount of emotional baggage that affects how he is in his intimate relationships.<br /><br />Although Christian has many issues stemming from emotional trauma as a child, and doesn't really know how to have a \\"normal\\" or what he calls a \\"vanilla\\" relationship,<br /><br />While Christian does some questionable things, is extremely controlling, and has a raging temper he doesn't seem to know how to contain at times, I was constantly left with the feeling that under all of his baggage, he's a truly decent man in so many ways. He and Ana butt heads throughout the story as he lavishes her with expensive gifts that she has an incredibly hard time accepting. In fact, his generosity is probably one of the biggest thorns in their relationship with her not wanting to take advantage of his wealth, and him not understanding why she can't accept what he can so obviously afford to buy her. He tends to be overprotective, and jealous, but even the way he comes across in those situations seem to stem from an honest place of deep caring. Being that \\"caring\\" is a word not previously in Christian's vocabulary, he spends a good amount of the story coming to terms with feelings he's never had for any other woman when it comes to Ana.<br /><br />Over the course of the book, the two exchange many emails. While the idea of two characters exchanging emails or texts throughout a story seems like an easy way to just fill page space, it was one of the things that made this story really enjoyable for me. Ana and Christian use email to open up to one another, something both have trouble doing early on; especially Ana. And the emails in this situation seem to force them to be honest to each other in their face to face conversations as well.<br /><br />The emails were also a way to see another side of Christian, a playful, flirty side that you don't always get to see on page when he's with Ana in person.<br /><br />Although this book starts out making you think it will be heavy on the D/s, as Christian is an intense Dom, looking to start a D/s relationship with Ana, it is actually very mild in that aspect, and really not what the focus of the story ends up being about.<br /><br />There are a couple minor issues I feel I should address. One is that Christian has such a dominant voice and presence in the story, that he seems far older than his given age of 27, not that a 27-year-old man can't be that dominant and sexy, it just took more effort for me to picture it. And there are a handful of over used phrases that became a bit overdone and a few British terms and phrases that just didn't fit with the \\"American\\" setting (the author is British).<br /><br />I've read many differing reviews about this book and it seems people either love it or hate it because of the way it's written or the fact that the author originally published it as a Fan Fiction story, etc. But any issues I had were overlooked easily enough thanks to a plot that, whether realistic to some or not, kept my interest from page to page so much that I literally couldn't put my Kindle down or stop thinking about the story when I wasn't reading, and I think I credit that to the character of Christian. He's such an intense presence in every single scene, that I fell head over heels with him and all of his baggage. I wanted to slap him at times and hug him at others...a feeling Ana is constantly faced with throughout the story.<br /><br />The end of this story was so intense and angsty (a major PLUS for me because I LOVE angst) that I was in tears by the end. Just a warning, however, I would have been extremely angry at the ridiculous cliffhanger if I didn't have the second book already waiting for me to read. So I suggest having book 2, FIFTY SHADES DARKER already in your possession before finishing book this book.<br /><br />Even though I say this story ends up being less about D/s than I had originally thought it would be, FIFTY SHADES OF GREY does contain a few scenes that the lighthearted might not enjoy, but overall, if you're a fan of say, Maya Banks' Sweet series, you should have no problem with this story. I could easily see myself reading it again or at least browsing through my many pages of bookmarks!<br /><br />I gave FIFTY SHADES OF GREY 4.5 Stars and 4 panties on the blog.ases that became a bit overdone and a few British terms and phrases that just didn't fit with the \\"American\\" setting (the author is British). I have no trouble with not having an \\"everybody lives and is happy ending\\".  Such an ending would have been a huge unrealistic cheat and I would have railed against that too. And you have no good sense of the overall picture - as another reviewer pointed out that's a weakness of the first person point of view but there's no reason you can't fill in the blanks while she's unconscious in the hospital over and over.  In the end, my biggest beef is the way Katniss just sort of happens through everything with so little meaning behind it or anything that she does.  She doesn't connect with the war movement, yet goes along with it.  It's not tragic, it's boring. I can think of 20 ways the execution/trial/fallout could have been done better than this.  The story needed a moving ending and instead it ends with a shrug.  Far too much of the story has to do with the war and not the people in it.  The first too books were all about the human stories, even when the Games were going on.  This war was not, and that's the real difference IMHO, and the main reason that this book just doesn't work.  I understand the point she's trying to make of how war is destructive on so many levels.  But this book doesn't make you feel the war, it just makes you feel Katniss' mind-numbing heartbreak/depression/numbness/injuries and it's so tedious.  Only once, when she starts training, do we get a TINY flash of the \\"wow she's going to do something big\\" because she cares again.  But then she just limps from scene to scene and we can care so little about what's going on.  She's a very flawed character I know, but here there's just no redeeming features to her at all.  No \\"aha\\" moments, very few flashes of brilliance.  Way too many injuries, hospitalizations, etc.  And too many times where she could resolve some things with dialogue but just goes silent.  So frustrating...  In the first two books the quiet, reflective parts add to the drama and flesh out the story. She didn't get any revenge on Snow (his death is an afterthought and should have been delivered with some satisfaction. Instead he gets the last word).  I could go on and on but there's no point.  This book pales in comparison to the first two and I'm really disappointed after loving the first two so much.

It's a film deep-rooted in the nostalgia that surrounds the game and it's rich history and harkens back to a simpler, more innocent time, when baseball was played for the love of the game. Unlike the steroid-laden and greed-driven abomination that the game has become today, baseball used to be something that was more than a game to the people of America. It was our life blood, a living, breathing embodiment of the American spirit. Somehow, that has gotten lost, but the root values are still there, somewhere, under all the muck and filth that the current version of the game has heaped upon it. That is what this film is about.<br /><br />This isn't simply just another \\"baseball\\" or \\"sports\\" film. It is a movie about characters and relationships. Not just the central figures of Ray Kinsella and \\"Shoeless\\" Joe Jackson, but of Rays patient and understanding wife Annie, their daughter Karen, Ray's father John, the embittered former writer and civil rights activist Terrence Mann (brilliantly portrayed by James Earl Jones), Annie's brother Mark, who plays the role of antagonist of this film, as well as Archie \\"Moonlight\\" Graham, the former ball player turned small town doctor who never got his shot to show what he could do, touchingly protrayed by film legend Burt Lancaster in one of his final roles.<br /><br />\\"Field of Dreams\\" is a movie that is sentimental; a poingnant, Norman Rockwell-esque painting of Americana set to celluloid. It is a movie of regret and retribution, a celebration of life and love that tugs at your heartstrings while simultaneously lifting your spirits. From the beautiful cinematography to the endearing and memorable soundtrack , the film is a gem, and is the best of the best in terms of baseball films (and sports films in general) and is an essential film on my top 50 list of all-time movies.

B0018QQQK8|3.0|I always like to start my reviews by giving a little bit about myself away. I'm a 17 year old male high school student who reads voraciously. I constantly vary my tastes from horror to mystery to literature to romance. I've never read Nicholas Sparks before, but I saw the movie [[ASIN:B000683VI4 The Notebook]] and literally balled my eyes out. So, I'm not exactly the target audience for Sparks' work, but I still felt like I should check it out.<br /><br />\\"The Lucky One\\" is about Logan Thibault, a US Marine who travels across the country in search of a mysterious woman in a photograph he believes to have been his lucky charm during his service in Iraq. There are a few twists and turns, but overall it's a straightforward tale and plays out pretty decently.<br /><br />And I think that's really the main flaw of this book: everything is just \\"decent\\" and \\"mediocre\\". It gets the job done. There's no risks, no shining prose, no super emotional stuff that really bonds you to the characters. Most things are taken at surface level. This isn't a bad thing if it's what you're looking for in a book. But this is not a deep, passionate, love story either.<br /><br />Nicholas Sparks isn't a wordsmith poet. He uses blessedly simple prose that tells the story fast and effectively. The only problem is, he's catering to an audience... or more likely what he thinks of an audience. Many subjects are broached upon by all of our three narrators, multiple times, one after the other. For example, when Protagonist A gets worried about their kid because Protagonist B is being a jerk and Protagonist C doesn't like it, we get to see and hear about it three times, from all three directions. This mildly interesting device very quickly wears thin and becomes tedious as the book goes on.<br /><br />The structure of the story also leaves a lot to be desired. It's starts out strong, the forward action only briefly interrupted by some expository flashbacks. But then, those flashbacks don't really stop... ever. Every time something interesting occurs in the present, Logan will need to relate it back to his days in the Marines, or his fishing trip with his friend, Vinny. This screws pacing up, a lot. So, then, by the middle of the book, when all of the plot threads start getting laid out, the exposition hasn't finished yet, and the middle really drags on. Luckily, everything picks up for the last 1/4 of the book, when real danger strikes and people start to actually do things.<br /><br />But the ending isn't perfect. By no means. It will satisfy even the most skeptical reader, because it wraps many things up in one swift move. But, at times, the climax does seem a little Soap Opera-ey, with characters responding to rumors and predictable, but still exciting developments playing out. Also, the Epilogue uses a cheap trick to make the reader feel any emotion at all. Tsk. Tsk.<br /><br />The characters in this book are probably its one flaw, with, of course, the delightful exception of Nana. Logan, Keith, and Ben are all basically one-trick ponies. They don't ever go too far into the land of archetypes, but they never evolve past cardboard static characters. Beth is a decent character, but not because she actually *does* much, more just because she has some active opinions and sometimes acts on them. The best character is Nana, who sounds the most human out of all of them and adds some much needed humor to this bland mixture.<br /><br />In conclusion, this was my first Nicholas Sparks novel, and it will be my last for a long time. I don't want to give up on him entirely, but I think my tastes are better suited elsewhere, at least for the time being. Maybe in a year or two, I'll pick up [[ASIN:0446605239 The Notebook]] or one of his newer novels, and give him another shot. For now, I'll stick to my other favorites.<br /><br />I Recommend this book to anyone who:<br />- Is from the South, specifically the Carolinas<br />- Loves Dogs<br />- Likes the classic idea of \\"family\\"<br />- Is happy when justice gets served to the bad guys<br />- Likes realistic dramaily\\"<br />- Is happy when justice gets served to the bad guys<br />- Likes realistic drama

0307352145|5.0|\\"Don't hire introverts!\\" the workshop leader said to a group of travel agents at a conference I attended in 2002.  He was giving advice on how to run a successful agency.  \\"Oh great,\\" I thought.  I had just left a 21 year career as a very introverted software engineer to enter the field of travel.  The prospects of me succeeding in this new venture seemed slim as I am by nature a quiet person.  I wish back then I had Susan Cain's excellent new book \\"Quiet: The Power Of Introverts in a World That Can't Stop Talking\\".  While that workshop leader and many others may think this is an extrovert oriented World, the number of introverts could surprise you.  \\"Depending on which study you consult, one third to one half of Americans are introverts--in other words, one out of every two or three people you know,\\" Cain writes. \\"If you're not an introvert yourself, you are surely raising, managing, married to, or coupled with one.\\"  \\"Quiet\\" reveals the value of the introvert personality type.  Cain says it took her five years to write the book and it shows.  \\"Quiet\\" is one of the best researched books I have ever read.  Cain draws from history, biographies, interviews she conducted, and her own personal experience to paint a rich picture of the quiet temperament.  I could relate to one passage about Stephen Wozniak, the co-founder of Apple Computer.  Cain quotes Wozniak in his book iWoz, \\"Most inventors and engineers I've met are like me--they're shy and they live in their heads,\\" Wozniak writes. \\"They're almost like artists. In fact, the very best of them are artists. And artists work best alone where they can control an invention's design without a lot of other people designing it for marketing or some other committee. I don't believe anything really revolutionary has been invented by committee. If you're that rare engineer who's an inventor and also an artist, I'm going to give you some advice that might be hard to take. That advice is: Work alone. You're going to be best ableto design revolutionary products and features if you're working on your own. Not on a committee. Not on a team.\\"  Cain's book is filled with little vignettes such as this, inspiring introverts like me.  More than a bunch of facts about introverts, Quiet is a fun book too.  I liked Cain's conversational writing style.  \\"Introverts .. may have strong social skills and enjoy parties and business meetings, but after a while wish they were home in their pajamas,\\" Cain says.  Isn't that the truth! (as I write this review in my pajamas :-) )  In chapter after chapter, Cain nails the introvert character type. \\"They prefer to devote their social energies to close friends, colleagues, and family. They listen more than they talk, think before they speak, and often feel as if they express themselves better in writing than in conversation. They tend to dislike conflict. Many have a horror of small talk, but enjoy deep discussions.\\"  If you are an introvert, \\"Quiet\\" will give you a better appreciation of your personality type, and insights on how to use this temperament to your advantage.  I especially liked the tips on how to avoid overextending myself in social situations.  If you are an extrovert, \\"Quiet\\" has value, too, as you will gain an understanding of the introverts in your life.

0553386794|4.0|First of his A Song of Ice and Fire series, George Martin's 'A Game of Thrones' is set in a brutally realistic and relentless world. It spans two continents; one setting is a typical pre-gunpowder medieval society with several alterations to the world, the second is based on Mongol culture. Martin doesn't hold back - these are harsh civilisations, highly misogynistic, and nobody is exempt.<br /><br />There are two main stories here, divided by the continents though with some small links between them. Both are, basically, political dramas, told in two seperate ways. We get eight viewpoint characters; seven of these are focused on the medieval-set plot that's predominant in this book, and just one perspective is involved in the Mongol-based society.<br /><br />There's a fine line between plot and characters here. On one hand, because it's a story about political intrigue it's necessarily character-driven, so there needs to be a focus on characterisation and it needs to be exemplary. On the other, there are certain strictures on whose viewpoints we see to keep everything running along, meaning that the perspectives we do get tend to have some limitations - we only see get a perspective when it has some sort of relevance to the overall plot, there's rarely a spare scene solely dedicated to the characters and their lives.<br /><br />I think, for the better part, Martin handles this very well. He's very punctual; his prose is competent and meant for communicating information smoothly, with little-to-no emphasis on language. The pace is set very well, the story isn't convoluted and we get what we need to see with very little in between. In every chapter there's something happening. There's twists and turns, tragedy and intrigue. Martin spares no-one from the cruelty of his world, and he often does this well, to have an effect that resonates through the rest of the story.<br /><br />The part I think he doesn't quite get right is characterisation. I found it a bit hit and miss - his characters have their own pasts, their own defining characteristics, and I found all of this believable, but with some characters I felt it was taken too much to an extreme, and this is where I had trouble believing the characters. I still feel a little unsure about it. While I was reading I didn't particularly notice it, but on reflection it sticks out.<br /><br />There's very little magic here, and what magic there is tends to break away from the ordinary fantasy tropes. We don't see much of it, lending to the realisation of this world, but it's obvious that several stereotypical fantasy things will make an appearance, whilst the more interesting magical revelations leave a sense of originality and intrigue.<br /><br />There's no hard finish here. Instead there's a semi-conclusive ending which clearly sets up the future books, much like others publishing multi-book tales. Nevertheless, the story is satisfying, with more than a few surprises which made the plot stand out. It hasn't quite gripped me yet, but if Martin continues in this vein then the rest of the series looks very promising.<br /><br />4.5/5, their own defining characteristics, and I found all of this believable, but with some characters I felt it was taken too much to an extreme, and this is where I had trouble believing the characters. Nevertheless, the story is satisfying, with more than a few surprises which made the plot stand out. It hasn't quite gripped me yet, but if Martin continues in this vein then the rest of the series looks very promising.  4.5/5

I bought the Back from Earth EP and was shocked again at the quality of the other songs. Bruno has surrounded himself with a group of talented musicians with great chemistry to make AWOLNATION into a full-fledged powerhouse of a band (I highly recommend seeing them live - I have twice and they were incredible both times).  As for the musical style of this album, AWOL takes the idea of a \\"genre\\" and headbutts it with an astronaut helmet. Electro, metal, pop, alternative rock, funk, industrial, hip-hop (even a surprisingly good guest rapper on the bonus track \\"Knights of Shame\\") can all be found mixed together to create something that makes you want to headbang, breakdance, chill out, and make love all within the span of a few seconds. It's almost impossible to describe AWOLNATION's \\"sound\\" other than in terms of quality: excellent. There isn't a song (not counting the intro and 2 interludes, tracks 1, 2 & 9) that I would rate less than 4 out of 5, and each has a completely unique sound. That being said, the most standout tracks in my opinion, in order, are \\"Guilty Filthy Soul,\\" \\"Knights of Shame,\\" \\"Sail,\\" \\"Soul Wars,\\" \\"Kill Your Heroes,\\" and \\"Not Your Fault.\\" But for only $9, you probably won't find a better deal than this entire album.

0156027321|4.0|Never are opinions more divided than when you talk books, that's why you read vile-filled 1-star reviews that marvel at the stupidity of those who would enjoy such a book listed side-by-side with the starry-eyed, glowing remarks of those who absolutely loved it. That is, unless you talk music. Or sports teams. Or...  Sometimes you just have to be in the right mood for the genre. Other times, the opinions stated in a book shouldn't rankle your nerves or clash with your own personal viewpoints and philosophies. Then there are also those moments that, if you don't like or understand a character, you're not going to care enough about what they do to bother reading to find out.  This is a book that centers around a single protagonist -- his worries, his joys, his struggles and his inspirations. You'll like it if you enjoy witnessing the conflicts and complications in one person's life because Pi is an interesting and sympathetic character, admirable because of how he manages to get through a desperate, grotesque situation. Yes, there is crass and gross descriptions of death and survival and all the mundane points which make up a day isolated at sea.  I was personally fascinated by how he managed to do it without going insane, interested in his memories and opinions and plans and schemes, and especially compelled to keep reading because I truly wanted to find out what happened.  The behavior of the tiger is also interesting... I also kept reading to unravel the mystery of its companionship with Pi. That unnatural partnership was different and therefore something new.  I like reading something new.  I enjoy survival stories, though, and have always been drawn to them. Although it isn't a true comparison, the basic premise in Life of Pi that is similar to that of The Girl Who Loved Tom Gordon, by Stephen King. Each has a combination of wonder, coming of age, and blunt survival. I listened to this as an audiobook rather than read it, and it felt as if I was sitting firesidewith Pi as he recounted his journey to me...that helped my being able to make a connection.  Personally, I did think the first few chapters were a bit too dry and \\"talky\\" with all the background details about the zoo and Pi's familial and religious upbringing, so I listened less intently to that part, but from the shipwreck on, I was hooked. By the end of the book, I was inspired because it felt like I shared a bit of triumph along with Pi, made it through a difficult experience and learned from it right along with Pi. Isn't that one of the greatest things about books? Being able to go places you'd never go and know people you'd never otherwise know and see things from a perspective you've never had before?  I've rated Life of Pi 4 out of 5 stars on its merits as literature: strong character development; well-structured story with the perfect balance of highs and lows, conflicts and complications; vividly descriptive language; and overall \\"production values\\" as it's put together cohesively. Its flaw is in the slow, dragging beginning which (in my opinion at least) doesn't completely jive with the rest of the book -- it's necessary background for understanding the character but I couldn't help but feel it could've been presented more succinctly or in a more active voice.  Those who bash the book with 1 star and call it \\"boring\\" are being unfair.  If survival stories with brutally blunt descriptions of life and death don't interest you, then this isn't the book for you. Reading such a book then saying it's a bad book would be like knowing you hate nuts, ordering a bowl of Hagan Dazs Butter Pecan ice cream then declaring that Hagan Dazs makes the worst ice cream you've ever tasted.  Life of Pi is, overall, perfect when you are in the mood for something both harrowing and inspired, with a satisfying ending that may make you look at your own life with a bit more appreciation.

Tom Sawyer is easily recognizale and has a futuristic style and sound to the music. This is mostly due to the influence of drummer Neil Peart who was the mastermind behind such Rush classics as A Farewell To Kings and 2112. His scientifically inspired themes became the foundation on which Rush built their collective legendary status as both muscians and songwriters. In addition, guitarist Alex Lifeson was always inspiring as a jazz/fusion guitarist. Bassist Geddy Lee, who is a unique vocal talent and stupendous bassist offered a great counterwieght to both Lifeson and Peart. Probably the second most famous track on the album is Limelight. The song is a more mature and reflective piece featuring many nuggets of wisdom. The albums most agressive track, Red Barchetta used a hot Italian sports car as inspiration. Peart's drumming is precise and devastating on the track, one of the standout tracks of his long and storied career as one of rocks greatest drummers. He also employs a electro-mechanical drum system during the solo section of this track. YYZ is a meandering \\"jam\\" session, Lee, Lifeson, and Peart engaging thier insturments with collective brillance. This album is a must have for any Rush fan, progressive metal fan(although the bands previous 3 releases are best in this genre), as well as any collection of early eighties metal.
