B002MQYOFW|5.0|For Christmas 2011, I got my 14 year old sister the three book, box set, of The Hunger Games.  After opening her present, she told me she'd read the first book for school and had really liked it.  She was happy to get the next two books and lent me the first one.  When I started the book, I was instantly reminded of a short story I read myself in school, The Lottery by Shirley Jackson.  In both stories, the community experiences loss by the unfortunate circumstance of a name being drawn. There are many similarities to these stories, however The Hunger Games blossoms into a tale where the reader is emotionally engaged and wanting more.  The story is told in first person, present tense, by the main protagonist, Katniss.  She is a strong, young woman, emotionally guarded, and the main care giver to her mother and younger sister.  They live in a post apocalyptic society that has been divided into 12 districts.  These districts are controlled by the Capitol.  Every year, the Capitol, in order to maintain dominance over the districts, hosts the Hunger Games, a brutal competition for survival of the fittest, where the last person standing wins.  The Hunger Games is to be televised for all to watch, entertainment for some, and a reminder to all of the power of the Capitol.  Once again, I found this book reminding me of a movie I once saw, The Running Man with Arnold Schwarzenegger.  If you can get past the cheese factor of the 80's and lots and lots of spandex, you will find this film tells the story of a man being put to his death after being wrongfully accused.  His death sentence is to be televised in a kind of arena-like, game show.  He is fighting for his life while world watches for entertainment.  Overall, I found this book to be engaging.  It was an easy read which flowed well and did not lag.  Characters such as Rue and Thresh invoked emotion, and I could really see the good in people even during the barbaric Hunger Games.  The protagonist, Katniss, and her fellowdistrict 12 companion, Peeta, were well paired as opposites, one wearing his heart on his sleeve and the other unable to open up and give herself a chance to feel.  I think the real power of this book is Katniss.  She is a strong, young female who finds herself in a terrible predicament and yet never backs down.  She is a great archer with real stamina who uses her knowledge and skills to survive often out witting or performing the other characters.  However, she is not cruel or backstabbing, and I can see a real kindness in her through her engagements with Rue and Prim.  She does not fall into the common stereotype of a love-sick, girly teen, who has to be cared for by her man.  On the contrary, she is the one who cares for Peeta who's struck with puppy love.  It's true at times Katniss can be guarded and may seem a little cold when it comes to self preservation, but her actions speak louder than words.  I also believe this story to be a good tool to open up dialog for younger readers.  This book speaks of the Capitol, a community plagued by the Cardinal Sins.  The Capitol has fallen victim to vanity, altering its inhabitants for unattainable perfection.  No one in the Capitol seems to know hunger or poverty.  Even the simplest of tasks are done for them by the Avox (a kind of servant).  There seems to be no concern for the people of the 12 districts, the people who provide the Capitol with everything they need.  In comparison to today, I would think the Capitol could be interchanged for The One Percent.  I am referring to the 2006 documentary by Jamie Johnson, who talks about the richest 1% of Americans who controlled 42.2% of the financial wealth of America in 2004.  Overall, I would recommend this book for young readers and adults.  The characters are written well and it's really nice to see a strong female character like Katniss really kick ass!  The book tells a good story, one mixed with right and wrong, making readers question what they would do if they found themselves caught as a tribute in the Hunger Games, victim to the Capitol.

0316017930|5.0|Gladwell's exposition of the context in which greatness occurs is at once mightily heartening and crushingly depressing. Heartening because we realize that successful people are made, not born, and any of us could be the next big force on the world stage. Depressing because it means we are subject to forces we cannot control, and cannot reverse if they impact us disadvantageously. Either way, it lets us plan for coming generations.<br /><br />Success, Gladwell says, doesn't come to particularly moral, industrious, or determined people. It comes to those placed, by individual circumstance and cultural context, in the right places. It comes to those prepared, like Bill Gates, who had unusual access to computers at a time when they were rare and cumbersome novelties. It comes to those aggressive in pursuing perfection, like the Beatles, whose Hamburg years served as a rock music boot camp.<br /><br />This does mean that even ambitious people won't necessarily succeed. I can't change my birthdate to come of age during some important cultural phenomenon. I can't change my roots to incorporate, for instance, the engineering demands of rural Chinese agriculture. But I can overthrow certain limitations: for instance, deference to authority, which might be nice at cocktail parties, where greater egalitarianism saves lives in crisis situations.<br /><br />Of course, there is still a place for individual initiative. Groundbreaking corporate lawyer Joe Flom was well placed by circumstances of birth, family, and opportunity, which got him in on the ground floor of Eighties-era cutthroat business. But he also embraced that opportunity, rather than letting it skid by. Gladwell does not disparage individual strengths, just places them in a situational framework.<br /><br />Gladwell does give hope, primarily to coming generations, in educational paradigms that empower children to overcome their limitations. I may not shed my WASPy upbringing and my specific range of opportunity, but I might prepare my kids for whatever opportunities might come their way. This puts boundaries around my future, which is a bit depressing, but it also means I don't have to pass those boundaries onto the next generation.<br /><br />This book is a poke in the eye to Ayn Rand types who want to believe we exist in a vacuum, standing or falling on our own virtues. And, as an educator, I find it quite exciting that I can open doors for others willing to walk through. We have the chance to provide coming generations with opportunities you and I never had. If we can stop lionizing individuals without recognizing their context, we have the chance to build a better world for our kids.portunities might come their way. 0525422943|2.0|So I approached WHERE SHE WENT with almost equal amounts trepidation and excitement. It's hard to follow up an ending like the ending of [[ASIN:014241543X If I Stay]]. In many respects, I felt like the mere existence of a sequel might mar the beauty of the first book. I just . . . I loved how it ended so much, I wasn't at all sure I wanted to know what happened next. And if you know me at all you know how hilarious that sounds. I always want to know what happens next. I regularly fill my life with beloved series I can't quit. But I am also an enormous admirer of standalone volumes, particularly those featuring a judicious and perfect ending. Such was the case with [[ASIN:014241543X If I Stay]]. But the tenor of my emotions for Mia and Adam by the end of that book ensured that I wouldn't be able to stay away from a follow-up in the end. Especially as that follow-up was set three years later. So I only lasted about 24 hours in between finishing the first book and plunging into the second.<br /><br />A warning: If you have not yet read [[ASIN:014241543X If I Stay]], I suggest doing so now before proceeding any farther. It really is worth it, and there is simply no way to discuss the sequel without ruining the surprise both books have in store. Also, the nature of my feelings regarding this one demand a discussion of the ending. So. You have been warned, yes?<br /><br />It's been three years since Mia awoke to find Adam there waiting for her. And, as happy as she was to see him (and he her), it turns out three years is more than enough time for everything to fall apart. At first, everything seemed fine. More than fine. She was awake. She was alive. She had Adam there to help her through her recovery and convalescence. She had an acceptance letter from Julliard. But none of those positives were enough in the end. They weren't enough to outweigh the loss of her family. They weren't enough to keep Mia in the town where it all happened a moment longer than she had to stay. And, though neither of them could have seen it coming, they were not enough to sustain her relationship with Adam. So Mia makes the command decision to break things off entirely and leave, leaving behind an utterly demolished and dumbstruck Adam. Over the course of the next three years, both of their lives change beyond measure, as Mia embarks on the concert career she's always wanted, and Adam is skyrocketed to stardom in a whirlwind of success and good fortune. But time catches up with them. And when they both find themselves in New York over the same 24-hour period, they are forced to deal with the detritus that is all that remains of who they once were.<br /><br />WHERE SHE WENT is the opposite of [[ASIN:014241543X If I Stay]] in every way. Where [[ASIN:014241543X If I Stay]] was quiet and elegantly somber, WHERE SHE WENT is chaotic and jaggedly painful. Where [[ASIN:014241543X If I Stay]] was told from Mia's self-contained, removed perspective, WHERE SHE WENT is told from Adam's very different point of view. And, unfortunately, where [[ASIN:014241543X If I Stay]] has a breathtaking ending, WHERE SHE WENT has an ending that left me with my mouth hanging open at how inadequate it was. I know it was the ending so many readers were hoping for, and the truth is I wanted that ending as well. Especially after I followed Adam around and watched with such sadness the person he had become, my heart practically bursting with sympathy for him. I loved Adam so much in this book. It's his story in every sense of things, and it's written incredibly well. I soaked up his version of events and how he never got over Mia and how all the riches life deposited in his lap weren't enough to compensate for the loss of love and friendship. Full of rage, sadness, and basically every negative, hurt emotion I can think of, he absolutely steals the show. Enter Mia and everything that has happened to her in the intervening years. I loved the setup, and I loved how it was simultaneously easyto be together again and awkwardly ruinous at the same time. From their halting physical proximity to their loaded verbal exchanges, everything was handled so well. I couldn't wait to get to the culmination of things and the inevitable decision they would have to make. And this is where we come to the inadequacy. I wanted that ending, but not this way. Not at the expense of my respect for the characters. After all of that, all of the thought and heartbreak and complexity that went into these two people who loved each other as kids and have surmounted tragedy to become adults all on their own, how could it wrap up so easily? How could either of them be satisfied with the meager resolution that was proffered? I wasn't. I wanted more for Adam and more from Mia, and that's all there is to it. All of that excruciating growth and hard-earned experience deserved more than a token apology on her part and a grateful acceptance on his. It was too fast, too simple, and too saccharine even for my romantic heart. I ended it in dismay, wishing it wasn't so. In other words--the exact opposite of how I felt at the end of [[ASIN:014241543X If I Stay]]. Each reader's reaction will be different, of course (see a myriad other reviews). This was mine and mine alone. I'm so very glad Mia chose to stay. I just really wish I hadn't found out where she went.ardly ruinous at the same time. 014241543X|4.0|It was all the hype surrounding the release of [[ASIN:0525422943 Where She Went]] that actually got me interested in reading IF I STAY. I was aware of the love it received upon its publication a couple of years ago. But even though it appeared to be universally loved, I never picked it up, as I tend to be somewhat leery of coma/out of body experience stories. It's not that I've read a ton of them, but in the few that I have read, I've had a hard time connecting to the main character. What with them being . . . well, comatose and all. So I'd pretty much decided I wasn't going to try this one, until the blogosphere had a collective meltdown over there being a sequel at all and then at how awesome it apparently was. You know how hard it is to resist that promise of a sequel that lives up to (possibly even surpasses) its predecessor. I'm certainly not able to hold out against it for long. So I snagged a copy of IF I STAY and ignored my niggling concerns to see just what all the fuss was about.<br /><br />Mia comes from a musical family. From her ex-rocker dad and groupie mom to her adorable drummer of a little brother, her family has music in its bones. And Mia does, too. But not in exactly the same way. You see, Mia loves classical music. She's a dedicated cellist and just waiting for that acceptance letter to Julliard. Her loving family supports her passion, if they remain a bit bemused at how straitlaced and determined she is. Then the unthinkable happens. Mia wakes up to find she's been in a car crash. Her entire family was in the car, and now she's in a coma and her parents and brother are . . . gone. From a foggy distance, Mia watches life go on and alternates between watching her body in the hospital and flashbacks to life before the crash, to her relationships with her family members and with her sweet musician boyfriend Adam. What will happen, Mia is not sure. Is there anywhere to go from here? Is she stuck in an eternal in-between? And most importantly, will she ever have the chance to tell her loved ones how much they meant to her?<br /><br />My emotions about IF I STAY have not faded over time. It's a monumentally quiet story, and the reader drifts along with Mia on a gently wistful refrain of loss. In fact, it's so quiet and gentle that it borders on bland. Which is not to say that I didn't like Mia and Adam and the rest. I did. Very much. And I'm not sure what I was expecting, but what I got was much lower key than that. The writing was competent and unobtrusive, the characters sympathetic, and the situation (naturally) absolutely heartbreaking. But I wasn't consumed. I wasn't so attached that I couldn't put it down. But I kept reading because I did feel fond of these characters, and I really did want to find out how Gayle Forman would resolve Mia's story. And here's where things get interesting, my friends. Because IF I STAY has the best ending I've read in some time. And I am not even a little bit joking. I am seven kinds of serious when I say that this book ramps up to one humdinger of a climax. It was so good it took my breath away. And it sneaks up on you like nobody's business, so that when it does happen you immediately look back on the whole in wonder. Because it really was more than the sum of its parts. It was one of those not just a good ending, but the only one, you know? And all I could think was how well-crafted it was, how seamless and inconspicuous, how softly it crept, all so that you could experience the sheer magnitude of that ending. Honestly, I was just so impressed. It was the perfect ending, and truthfully I wasn't sure at all that I wanted a sequel. Which is actually how you ought to feel after finishing any book, isn't it? Bravo, Ms. Forman.ve the chance to tell her loved ones how much they meant to her?  My emotions about IF I STAY have not faded over time. If he continues to grow as a songwriter hopefully towards topics that feel less navel gazing and more relatable, he could become a great artist. As it currently stands he's still the only \\"pop superstar\\" making music right now that i would care to return to. I would openly recommend this album to those who shun pop music and tend to find it lacking. There's a wealth of solid material on this, that i'd say any music fan should check out.<br /><br />Take Care ;)

0099908506|3.0|Largely biographical, Fiesta is about a group of American men in Paris and their entanglements with Brett Ashley, an English socialite, who has a complex love relationship with protagonist Jake Barnes.  Hemingway's non-committal style and spare prose leaves Jake in control of the narrative of which he is central but unobtrusive, revealing less about the events (which take them to the Spanish Fiesta) in the book than about Jake himself. Jake may be tacit in his narration, but the reader pieces together his frustrated feelings for Brett in a relationship that cannot be consummated, and her insatiable need to do just that by proxy with other men.  In the background, the dispiritedness and displacement of Jake and his friends reflective of Americans like Hemingway who found themselves drifting in the post-war years searching for meaning in European cities (called `The Lost Generation' by Gertrude Stein). This is a novel about love and loss, as much as it is a document of Paris in the 1920s.

Rothfuss makes it an easy transition. We learn from the beginning that Kvothe is an intelligent kid. He's lead into a series of nasty events that leave him in a life and death situation for much of the first half of the book. It reminded me of Brandon Sanderson's ability to produce a new and distinct kind of magic. Rothfuss planned it out well and details its properties using magical, but more often, scientific reasoning. Those who enjoy the world and magic building process will be happy with what Rothfuss has to offer in his first book.<br /><br />If somebody forced me to pick something I didn't like, I can only think of one possibility. There are two minor characters who spend a lot of time together and I had a tough time distinguishing between the two. Rothfuss might have been able to make each more unique as to set them apart. I hate to mention it because it wasn't really a big deal. I just remember some of their interactions being the only time I felt pulled from the story.<br /><br />A great book and creative enough to set itself apart from others in the genre.something I didn't like, I can only think of one possibility. B00005JNEW|5.0|Friday Night Lights is a 2004 drama film which documents the coach and players of a high school football team and the Texas city of Odessa that supports and is obsessed with them. The movie follows the story of the 1988 Permian High School Panthers football team as they made a run towards the state championship for the entire 1988 season, which culminated in a loss in the State championship against Carter High School from Dallas, but would have their title stripped for playing an ineligible player. However, the book also deals with -- or alludes to -- a number of secondary political and social issues existing in Odessa, all of which share ties to the Permian Panthers football team. These include socioeconomic disparity; racism; segregation (and desegregation); and poverty.<br />The coach, Gary Gaines (Billy Bob Thornton), is constantly in the hot seat. Tied to the successes and failure of the coach and the team in general are the conflicts the players struggle with on and off the gridiron. The coach overuses his star player and running back James \\"Boobie\\" Miles (Derek Luke) who gets seriously injured (Miles tore his ACL, missed the playoffs, and had a limp for the rest of his life). When this happens, sports radios are flooded with calls for his resignation. Miles' once-arrogant attitude vanishes as he sees his once promising chance of playing big-time college football disappear and starts to question his future after he notices his not-so promising academic standing. Quarterback Mike Winchell (Lucas Black) struggles with being able to play consistently. Fullback Don Billingsley (Garrett Hedlund) has a rocky relationship with his father (Tim McGraw), who won a state championship at Permian and carries on a feud with his son for not performing on the level he'd like to see, despite the fact that Don doesn't do anything to light his father's temper. His obsession with fame and recognition also comes at a high price that he is at first not ready to pay. B006UG90RM|5.0|Ok I admit it, when word first began to leak out about a new Van Halen album, one that miracle among miracles would feature Diamond Dave, I had my doubts. Could you blame me? After all, it was rock's worst kept secret how much acrimony had existed between Eddie Van Halen and David Lee Roth since Roth had left the band in 1985. If anything, the ensuing years only seemed to prove that things hadn't changed. Sure, Van Halen went on to have even more success with Sammy Hagar, while Diamond Dave had an ok but unspectacular solo career, but the likelihood of the original band ever recording again? A snowball not melting in hell seemed more likely than that.  Of course, there was another acrimonious break up (Hagar left the band in 1996, and then original bassist Michael Anthony), and Eddie's well known continuing drug and alcohol problems, as well as seemingly failing health. These events seemed to permanently put Van Halen (despite the Rock and Roll Hall of Fame induction) into the permanent place of most legendary bands: the occasional reunion tours (and there have been a few) to sold out crowds of fans who long for the days when rock and roll was actually fun and had actual bands that kids like me grew up wanting to be. The idea of a band like Van Halen coming out with anything noteworthy in 2012 (considering the guys were in their late 50s/early 60s) was hardly something to be taken seriously. I admit, I was getting sick and tired of all the rumors that began circulating about recording sessions, and new music coming out, only to find out that it was just a tease.  That's what makes 'A Different Kind of Truth' such a shock to the system. What immediately struck me was how hard this album ROCKS. There is nary a ballad or a keyboard to be found anywhere. A remarried, clean and sober Eddie plays his guitar with a ferocity that hasn't been heard since at least '1984'. Diamond Dave may not have the voice that yelped and screeched like the Van Halen of old, but on this album his vocals fit like a well worn pair of jeans, he doesn't try to over sing, and the trademark smart ass lyrics are still in heavy supply. While Michael Anthony's distinctive high harmony vocals are missed, Eddie's son Wolfgang is a more than competent bassist, so Anthony's absence is hardly noticed.  The album opening 'Tattoo' was an obvious first single choice, and probably the 'safest' song on the record, which features a great laid back groove and catchy lyrics about 'sexy dragon magic'. 'She's the Woman' might be recognizable to die hard VH fans as an earlier form of another classic (Mean Street). This was one of the bands early demos they cut after they were first signed, with Roth reworking some of the lyrics. Another song that fans might recognize a familiar riff (and this writer's personal fave) is 'Stay Frosty' which is basically 'Ice Cream Man' on steroids and features Roth musing about the world of preachers and other 'advice givers'. Another standout track is 'You and Your Blues' where Dave name checks some classic blues songs 'no Red House over yonder, no Stormy Monday blues' and sings about being brought down by someone who is a classic 'Debbie Downer' type.  There are some incredibly aggressive, fast paced rockers like 'As Is', 'China Town', and 'Honeybabysweetiedoll' which feature Eddie's trademark lightning fast riffing, the last of which goes by so fast, you almost miss it. He seems clearly inspired, and Roth seems to have brought out Eddie's inner id, which clearly had been missing on Van Halen's last few albums. Even at almost 60 years old, he still sounds and plays like no one else.  Whether or not this album leads to more music remains to be seen, but it's nice to know that in 2012 despite the 'gloominess' of the current rock scene that some old timers can show the young kids how you REALLY rock. After all, it's supposed to be fun!

B0059XTUB8|3.0|*** This review may contain spoilers ***<br /><br />Rating: 3.5<br /><br />Colin Clark was only 24 years old when he served as '3rd Assistant Director' on the 1957 film 'The Prince and The Showgirl' starring Marilyn Monroe and Lawrence Olivier. Clark kept a diary while on the set and later published it, along with a memoir of his purported experiences with Monroe. It's notable that Clark didn't seem overly impressed with the Hollywood icon, when he wrote in his diary, but when the memoir came out, his view of her apparently had changed to much more 'favorable'.<br /><br />For those unfamiliar with 'My Week with Marilyn', it provides a glimpse at Marilyn Monroe at the height of her popularity, a few months after she married famed playwright, Arthur Miller, in 1956. This was a chance for Marilyn to show her chops, pitted against stage legend Olivier, who in turn was given the reins to direct, 'The Prince and the Showgirl', a light weight comedy with the \\"most popular woman in the world\\", in the starring role. Dougray Scott is a dead ringer for Miller but his character is only on screen very briefly, as Miller decided to go back home right before the shoot, as he was having trouble putting up with all of Marilyn's mood changes. Judi Dench is also too briefly seen as Dame Sybil Thorndike, who plays an older aristocratic woman in the film within a film.<br /><br />Michelle Williams does a very nice job conveying the many moods of the starlet. When Monroe first came to the set of 'The Prince and the Showgirl', she was intimidated by Olivier, who was not impressed with her coach, Paula Strausberg, an advocate of Method Acting. While Kenneth Branagh doesn't look at all like Olivier, he ably conveys his contempt for both Marilyn's lack of professionalism and 'The Method' itself, as he was convinced that actors could never be spontaneous when utilizing such a technique. Olivier is seen warning Clark that he should not be fooled by Monroe's \\"little girl lost\\" routine. Not only was Monroe habitually late on the set but we learn of a more deep-seated insecurity, which she attempted to mask by imbibing huge amounts of prescription medication.<br /><br />Williams also does well in conveying Marilyn Monroe's charm. Most of the film's narrative is about how Colin Clark acts as her buffer, 'protecting' her against the various people she felt assailed by, including her business partner, Milton Greene, who attempted to bar Clark from spending time with her, during the production. Clark appears to fall completely for Monroe especially after she continuously flirts with him. In one scene, Monroe encourages Clark to join him skinny-dipping in a lake, off the beaten path, in the English countryside; later, she invites him into her bed for some heavy duty cuddling.<br /><br />While entertaining, the film suffers from the limits of its venue. We pretty much realize that the stakes aren't that high here to begin with--a young man takes a soft fall, after he realizes that his infatuation was just that--an infatuation! The 'dark moment' of the second Act arrives when Marilyn has her big meltdown, which threatens to shut down the entire production. All's well that ends when, when Marilyn wakes up and wants to go home for her mother-in-law's chicken soup, back in Brooklyn.<br /><br />Eddie Redmayne may be a tad too old to play Colin Clark, but is still fine as the impressionable gopher. Emma Watson is seen for a short time in a throwaway part as Colin's love interest, perhaps the least interesting role in the entire film. There are other players in short bits here, that add to the film's overall verisimilitude.<br /><br />Perhaps the most telling scene in the film is when Clark asks Marilyn to give up her career and run away with him. She graciously declines by replying that she enjoys her life. If that's the case, why does she spend so much time popping pills and generally being so down on herself? Olivier sees her as a lot shrewder than many of her handlers and sycophants, who appear to indulge and enable her. This is perhaps one aspect of Marilyn's character, the shrewd businesswoman, that is not explored very fully in this film--however, since we're only getting a glimpse of what she was like during a limited time period, such an exploration may be a bit beyond the scope of this overall project.<br /><br />'My Week with Marilyn' sometimes borders on hagiography. Whatever talent Marilyn Monroe did have, it seems that the many men who 'fell in love' with her, fell more for an image than a real person. I wonder if she could have done as well in the acting department, if she wasn't completely addicted to all her pills. Nonetheless, here, Michelle Williams does a fine job in serving up enough aspects of Marilyn Monroe's character to keep us interested until the end. Sadly Colin Clark is no longer with us, but while he was alive, he must have looked back on those heady days of his youth, as perhaps the greatest experience of his life. 'My Week With Marilyn' allows us to take that journey too, and a good part of it rings true.ect of Marilyn's character, the shrewd businesswoman, that is not explored very fully in this film--however, since we're only getting a glimpse of what she was like during a limited time period, such an exploration may be a bit beyond the scope of this overall project. I wonder if she could have done as well in the acting department, if she wasn't completely addicted to all her pills. Nonetheless, here, Michelle Williams does a fine job in serving up enough aspects of Marilyn Monroe's character to keep us interested until the end. Sadly Colin Clark is no longer with us, but while he was alive, he must have looked back on those heady days of his youth, as perhaps the greatest experience of his life. 'My Week With Marilyn' allows us to take that journey too, and a good part of it rings true.

0679722610|4.0|The Continental Op who is the narrator of the novel doesn't even give a name. If you know the author's history, he was probably basing it on his experiences with the Pinkerton Detective Agency in Butte, Montana. Anyway, the Op is sent to Personville which was pronounced Poisonville somewhere near Utah.<br /><br />The setting is curious to me. When you first start reading, you keep asking yourself why the Op stays in this corruptive village in the middle of nowhere. It's a mining town but it's filled with plenty of corruption. Everybody is corrupt even the narrator. The stories here were told in a serialized style for pulp fiction magazines for entertainment value.<br /><br />Is there literary value in pulp fiction, you bet there is with Dashiell Hammett who was the king of pulp fiction of his time period. He was a troubled author with alcohol problems, health, and his own demons of war and experiences as a Pinkerton detective. He was really trying to redefine the mystery detective genre.<br /><br />Unlike Sherlock Holmes, Jane Marple or Hercule Poirot, Op is unique in his voice. He's not necessarily evil nor good but shady and complicated. His character is never really defined nor do we do much about his character's history and background. He stays to clean up Personville.<br /><br />True to style, Hammett's writing includes plenty of references to alcohol during the prohibition time period which this book was written in the first place. It was published in 1926 at the height of prohibition where alcohol consumption and distribution was largely forbidden. Well, corruption ran rampant anyway.<br /><br />That's the morality tale here about corruption in a small town or any town over alcohol, power, money, etc. The characters here are thinly veiled and just mere caricatures of society's depiction of the time period. We don't feel sympathy towards anybody in this novel nor do we root for anybody even Op.<br /><br />I was assigned to read this novel for my 20th Century American history class in college about the 1920s. At times, I struggled with all the murders, crimes, motives, and explanations regarding the novel. I do now understand a lot more about the 1920s. Personville seemed more like Poisonville after all with blood, poison, manipulation, lies, treachery, even in the small towns.

B005LAJ23A|1.0|I happily bought the first season on blu-ray, but i will not spend a dime on the second season, purely an emo soap opera. What a let down. One can hope AMC will get back to Frank Darabont vision....The Walking Dead's second season premiered last fall to huge ratings and a fan response even bigger than the series premiere back in 2010. But according to former showrunner Frank Darabont, those same AMC budget cuts that helped drive him from the series also prevented his awesome idea to start season two with a prequel episode that would have put us inside a zombie war zone.  In The Walking Dead's first season we got a good look at the ruined city of Atlanta and the zombies that still occupied it as Rick Grimes (Andrew Lincoln) went searching for his family and found another gang of survivors instead. One of the more memorable sequences from the storyline found Rick trapped in a tank with a zombie soldier, who you might have recognized as Being Human's Sam Witwer.  It looks like an amusing little cameo from an actor Darabont worked with on The Mist, but in a video interview posted Jan. 3, Witwer revealed that his brief appearance was actually the setup for a potential prequel story.  \\"If you look closely, I played that zombie, because we were setting up this prequel we were going to do. If you watch the pilot of The Walking Dead, that's me in the tank as the zombie, and then Rick blasts him and he gets deafened, and he gets that grenade which saves him at the end of the season,\\" Witwer said.  The prequel revolving around Witwer's zombie soldier was, predictably, nixed because of budget cuts. He may have a sweet gig on Being Human these days, but Witwer's still not happy that AMC let Darabont and all his awesome ideas go.  \\"It's not happening now. Why? Because AMC wanted to save a few bucks. That is just one example of the kind of cool, awesome forethought [Darabont] has put into this show that is now absolutely written off,\\" Witwer said. \\"For me it doesn't matter much, because I'm busy doing Being Human. We were going to schedule things around. I'm not lamenting the loss of a job, I'm lamenting the loss of an amazing idea. And there are dozens and dozens of amazing ideas just like that which are now gone.\\"  CraveOnline initially reported that the concept was going to be used for a web series, but when Darabont responded to the story in a letter to Ain't It Cool News, he said it was actually the plan for the whole season-two premiere.  \\"The idea was to do this with a very focused 'you are there' documentary feel. Not going all shaky-cam, but still making it a bit rawer and grainier than the rest of the show,\\" Darabont said. \\"We'd start with a squad of maybe seven or eight soldiers being dropped into the city by chopper. They have map coordinates they need to get to; they've been told to report to a certain place to provide reinforcement. It's not a special mission, it's basically a housekeeping measure putting more boots on the ground to reinforce key intersections and installations throughout the city. And we follow this group from the moment the copter sets them down.\\"  Basically the idea was to do a kind of Saving Private Ryan or Black Hawk Down with zombies. What starts as a simple mission would have all gone to hell, but over the course of the episode we would have also had a chance to run into a few familiar faces.  \\"Picture our squad arriving at a manned barricade where some civilians are being held back from leaving the city on shoot-to-kill orders to stop the spread of contagion, it's a panicked high-intensity scene, and in this crowd of desperate people we find Andrea and Amy,\\" Darabont said. \\"The barricade gunners panic, the civilians start to get mowed down by machine gun fire, and in this melee the girls get pulled to safety by some old guy they don't even know. It's Dale. He's nobody to them, just some guy who saw the opportunity to do the right thing and reacted in the moment. This would have been perhaps a minute ortwo of the episode, just a cool detour like the various outposts the soldiers encounter in Saving Private Ryan, but we would have witnessed the moment that Dale meets Andrea and Amy, seen where that relationship began.\\"  The episode would have ended with Witwer's character as the last man standing, reaching the coordinates the squad was supposed to hit even though he'd already been bitten by the undead. He crawls into the tank, prepared to commit suicide by grenade, then dies instead. Darabont then wanted to time-lapse into a shot-for-shot reprise of Rick entering the tank and encountering Witwer's zombie.  \\"The notion was to take the 'throwaway' tank zombie Rick encountered in the pilot, and tell that soldier's story,\\" Darabont said. \\"Make him the star of his own movie, follow his journey, but don't reveal who he is until the end. The idea being that every zombie has a story, every undead extra was once a human being with a life of his/her own ... was, in a sense, the star of his own life's movie. And we've followed this one particular guy and seen how his life ended; we witness his struggles, see his good intentions and his failures, and we experience his godawful death in this tank. That's why I cast Sam as that tank zombie in the first place instead of just casting some extra. I had this story in mind while filming the pilot, and I knew I'd need a superb actor to play that soldier when the time came.\\"  Darabont also noted that the idea of a \\"wild card\\" episode focusing on a character we're not used to meeting was one he would have liked to continue as the series progressed. But of course, none of that will happen now. Darabont is gone and the show goes on, but at least we can think about what might have been.

B0071T5PN0|4.0|Van Halen has always been a band that has found a way to break traditional rules about popularity and longevity and forge their own path in the music business. In many ways this record continues that tradition. I have to be honest here and say, the first time I heard this record, I was not exactly elated. When you read about a new Van Halen project with David Lee Roth, I think a natural expectation is to hear a record loaded with radio hits. But in truth that is a lofty and unfair rationale given their last group effort with Dave took place in the 80's (a full record, not just individual tunes here and there).<br /><br />So is ADKOT a followup to 1984? Not even close. Is it more related to Diver Down or Women and Children First? Not exactly. Does it sit closer to Fair Warning? A few tunes find more common ground there. But overall I really have to say ADKOT is it's own animal. From what I have read, the majority of these are rewritten songs originally conceived and demoed back in the 70's and early 80's. So the album certainly weaves itself in and out of those records as you listen to the various treatments. But there is definitely a stamp of today in those songs.<br /><br />It's hard to know what the wisdom is in going back and reworking songs from a different era that never made it on a record. Does the era really dictate much in the final product since they are being reworked towards the sensibilities of the artist today? You're definitely waxing nostalgia for a specific demographic when that becomes part of the selling point. But for me, this album honestly comes across more as Van Halen today. And the references to yesterday are really not relevant to that final product.<br /><br />What IS surprising is how much the record grew on me. And that in itself is a different listening experience with Van Halen. Instead of hearing instant favorites, I slowly warmed to many of the songs to the point where I really like them today. &#34;Shes the Woman&#34; and 'Stay Frosty&#34; are easily among my personal favorites. &#34;Tattoo&#34; while an odd choice as a lead single, does retain enough appeal to hold it's own from the Van Halen hit factory. The album as a whole is a considerable jam session that makes me appreciate the talent that is Eddie Van Halen. The listening experience has also taught me that sometimes its better to let these artists come to you on their own terms rather than blindly hand over the same old predictable product.<br /><br />Eddie Van Halen continues to push the boundaries and create music the way he hears it. And while this record did not immediately warm to me like previous efforts, it definitely made me appreciate Eddie as an artist and remind me he is forever trying to find new and inventive ways to bring new material to the masses. Sometimes the music purposely veers from becoming predictably commercial, but it's always inventive and unrelenting in finding a new direction. That's a refreshing quality that seems to forever define this artist and his band. I have tremendous respect for that.ty&#34; are easily among my personal favorites. &#34;Tattoo&#34; while an odd choice as a lead single, does retain enough appeal to hold it's own from the Van Halen hit factory. 0142419400|5.0|So a moment of truth. I would never, ever, ever have picked this book up and given it a shot if I hadn't first heard all the references to it by EVERY BLOGGER ON THE NET. No, my caps lock is not broken. I feel confident in saying that this book is universally loved and adored by everyone who reads it. Seriously. I have yet to see one bad review. I first became aware of Anna and the French Kiss while stalking the YA book blogs last summer. At that time, I was firmly planted in the YA paranormal reads camp. I was beginning to branch out more and was reading some dystopic/post apocalyptic, but I absolutely did not do contemporary YA lit. I am embarrassed to admit that in my mind I felt contemporary YA was too much on the young side, not enough on the adult side. Even though I had loved them at the time, I hadn't read contemporary teenage books since I was a teenager.  Had I seen this book sitting on a shelf in a book store I would have passed it right on by. I mean look at that title for goodness sake. It SCREAMS teenager! I thought, as a thirty something, that I wouldn't be able to relate. Well, I was wrong. On all fronts. Today, a majority of what I read is contemporary YA. And the book bloggers whose reviews I was reading were all recommending Stephanie Perkins' Anna and the French Kiss. I am so thankful that I followed their advice.<br /><br />What makes this book so utterly fabulous? Let me count the ways. Characters, characters, characters. Authentic, familiar dialogue. Romance that will set your heart a flutter. And an enchanting setting that becomes a character unto itself.<br /><br />Let's begin with our protagonist Anna. Has there ever been a more genuinely likeable character than Anna Oliphant?  She's got so much going on. Here she is, the ultimate fish out of water, a southern girl spending her senior year in Paris. She doesn't speak the language. She's never been this far away from home or her family. She's missing her best friend and she's bummed that she was just starting to connect with her long time crush /coworker when her nouveau rich dad decides to ship her off and add some culture to her life.<br /><br />\\"At least the people in my new school speak English. It was founded for pretentious Americans who don't like the company of their own children. I mean, really. Who sends their kids to boarding school? It's so Hogwarts. Only mine doesn't have cute boy wizards or magic candy or flying lessons.\\"<br /><br />But other then a few bouts of crying in the beginning, Anna bounces back. She's tough, she can handle it. And I love that. We know Anna feels scared, anxious, and sad through the awesome inner monologue Perkins writes for her. Sometimes it pours out in a jumbled neurotic rant, which is fun to read and I think pretty darn true to life. Hasn't everybody gone off in their own head a few times (or perhaps on a fairly regular basis if you are like me) when life throws a bunch curve balls your way? It's these things that make Anna so real and authentic. It's easy to root for her and get behind her as she struggles to find her place in her new surroundings. Another thing that appeals to me about Anna is her moral compass. She is very sensitive and very considerate of her friend's feelings. When faced with a choice, she looks at all the angles and (for the most part) makes the right decision for herself. And you know, it's really nice to see a female MC who is not falling all over herself and looking like a fool for the attention of the boy she likes. Other than their first meeting, she is almost always composed around Etienne St.Clair. It seems like every heroine in YA these days does so much tripping and stuttering around cute boys, it's an embarrassment to my gender girls! C'mon, stand tall and hold your own like Anna does. If she can remain composed around someone like St. Clair, ANYONE can.<br /><br />Which brings us to the man himself. Oh, St. Clair. Stephanie Perkins really outdid herself when she crafted St. Clair. He's not perfect. He's short. He bites his nails to the nub. He's scared of heights AND scared of change.  He's got some issues. But in his own imperfect way, he is absolutely perfect. He's kind, he's funny, he's got great hair, and let's not forget that accent.<br /><br />\\"Eh-t-yen says my name like this: Ah-na.<br />My heart thump thump thumps in my chest.\\"<br /><br />The romance builds ever so slowly as St. Clair (with the long term girlfriend) and Anna (with the giant, unrequited crush back in Atlanta) become not just friends, but the best of friends. Like When Harry Met Sally friends. That's not to say that their aren't some moments between them that will make you tingle from your head to your toes, because there ARE.<br /><br />\\"It's odd, but I keep finding myself distracted. By the white of his teeth through the darkness. By a wavy bit of his hair that sticks straight out to the side. By the soft aroma of his laundry detergent. He nudges me to silently offer the armrest, but I decline and he takes it. His arm is close to mine, slightly elevated. I glance at his hands. Mine are tiny compared to his large, knuckly boy hands.<br />  And suddenly I want to touch him.<br />  Not a push, or a shove, or even a friendly hug. I want to feel the creases in his skin, connect his freckles with invisible lines, brush my fingers across the inside of his wrist. He shifts. I have the strangest feeling that he's as aware of me as I am of him. I can't concentrate. The characters on the screen are squabbling, but for the life of me, I don't know what about. How long have I not been paying attention?<br />  St. Clair coughs and shifts again. His leg brushes against mine. It stays there. I'm paralyzed. I should move it; it feels too unnatural. How can he not notice his leg is touching my leg? From the corner of my eye, I see the profile of his chin and nose, and -oh, dear God,- the curve of his lips.<br />  There. He glanced at me. I know he did.<br />  I bore my eyes into the screen, tyring my best to prove that I am Really Interested in this movie. St. Clair stiffens but doesn't move his leg. Is he holding his breath? I think he is. I'm holding mine. I exhale and cringe- it's so loud and unnatural.<br />  Again. Another glance. This time I turn, automatically, just as he's turning away. It's a dance, and now there's a feeling in the air like one of us should say something. Focus, Anna. Focus. \\"Do you like it?\\" I whisper.<br />  He pauses. \\"The film?\\"<br />  I'm thankful the shadows hide my blush.<br />  \\"I like it very much,\\" he says.<br />  I risk a glance, and St. Clair stares back. Deeply. He has not looked at me like this before. I turn away first, then feel him turn a few beats later.<br />  I know he's smiling, and my heart races.\\"<br /><br />Sigh.<br /><br />As we are left to wonder \\"will they, or won't they?\\" we learn more about Anna and St. Clair as well as their tight group of friends, described by Anna as having \\"an abundance of stylish black clothing and deliberately mussed hair\\" revealing they belong to the resident art clique. Each add so much to this story. I feel we get real insight into them, they aren't just extra bodies taking up space between the Anna/St.Clair story. I love Mer for taking Anna under her wing and assimilating her into her group, and I empathize with her in her own unrequited crush. I love the back and forth between Josh and Rashmi. And I love discovering more about them as Anna does. And Bridgette. I love her and wanted to strangle her at the same time. I don't think her friendship with Anna should be overlooked, because it really makes Anna take a step back and look at her own actions. Perkins does an amazing job with all of her characters, and I would love to see a spin off series with each of them (I think we do get more Josh in her third and final book of the series, Isla and the Happily Ever After, due to be released in 2013.) Lots of love in that paragraph, friends.  Literally. I said love four times. But it's true. I've fallen in love (five) with this book and this writer and and I must shout it from the Paris rooftops:)<br /><br />Which leads me to the final character featured in Anna, the city of Paris itself. So I'm lucky enough to have traveled there with my dad when I was in college. And it was all sorts of wonderful. But, I have often times wished I could go again, this time with my husband, and explore the city more with someone I loved. Not that I don't love my dad, but I mean looovve, like Anna/ St. Clair love. Because it IS the most romantic city in the world. Even traipsing around as a tourist with my old man,  I could see that. If I ever go back I'd skip all the tourist sites and most of the museums (I am an art major, cut me some slack) and I'd eat more and drink more better. I'd go in the spring (I went during Christmas back in college) and discover all the gardens and fountains. I'd go back to Notre Dame and look for the Point Zero stone in the street and make a wish as Anna and St. Clair did. Perkins could write for Frommer's or Lonely Planet travel guides (though it would be a waste of talent) because she's put so many wonderful details and visual imagery into her descriptions of this city. We can hear the opera singer across from Anna's dorm singing. We can taste the brioche with pears and pastel colored macarons. We can feel the cobblestone street under our feet as we run through the night. As she has stated in interviews, Perkins has written a love letter to Paris with this book and I applaud her.<br /><br />So once again, thank you fellow reviewers and bloggers. Without your pearls of wisdom I would never have taken a chance on this absolutely lovely piece of contemporary YA fiction and would have been really missing out. You girls have given me some great advice on books and authors I might not have discovered on my own (like Stephanie Perkins and Maureen Johnson and Simone Elkeles and Melina Marchetta and John Green and....and....) and I am eternally grateful. And thank you Stephanie Perkins for creating these amazing characters and this heartfelt story. You have set the bar high and I am looking forward to reading your stories for many years to come.esn't move his leg. Is he holding his breath? I think he is. I'm holding mine. B0028RXXE8|4.0|A friend recommended this series, stating \\"you can't stop watching\\". Like the book that keeps you up all night reading, this HBO series of Julius and Augustus Caesar captures your interest.<br /><br />Howbeit, the series has serious shortcomings. It doesn't hold up to repeated viewing. Second, the colloquial British diction detracts from the action if you aren't British. Third, the domestic problems of Lucius Vorenus are insipidly melodramatic and tiresome. A fourth shortcoming is the abusive Forrest Gump theme: two simple Roman soldiers (Lucius Vorenus and Titus Pullo) repeatedly appear at crucial moments in Roman history and unwittingly exert immense influence: (eg, \\"How Titus Pullo Brought Down the Roman Republic\\", etc). While these two characters are very believable as portrayed, their deus ex machina appearances throughout twenty years of Roman history simply are not believable and are improvidently overdone. Such fusion of zany black humor with otherwise serious history *could* impart charm, but the humor is so overdone that it contaminates by dramatic osmosis other scenes that demand a serious tone. (Imagine Saving Private Ryan peppered throughout with brief snippets of McHale's Navy, or recall Jar Jar Binks in George Lucas's Star Wars prequels.)<br /><br />On the plus side is the graphic portrayal of pre-Christian pagan values: These are hard people without sentiment for whom sudden death and chronic mayhem are daily occurrences taken in stride. One might meaningfully say they are spiritually dead people. Whether ancient Romans really were this scorpion-like and amoral is problematic, of course, but it's certainly refreshing to see a genuine attempt to depict an ancient culture *without* modern Humanist/Christian values.<br /><br />The casting, overall, is superb. The characters are lifelike depictions consonant with Plutarch's Lives, Shakespeare's plays, and the historical record generally. Although Julius Caesar obviously is whitewashed into near-sainthood, you nevertheless can imagine you really are encountering the great man's spirit through the inspired acting of Ciaran Hinds. David Bamber as Cicero and James Purefoy as Mark Antony are doubly superb.<br /><br />Again, while I don't feel Rome holds up to repeated viewing, this series is pretty amazing nonetheless. If all films were of this caliber, it would be a very different world., you nevertheless can imagine you really are encountering the great man's spirit through the inspired acting of Ciaran Hinds. David Bamber as Cicero and James Purefoy as Mark Antony are doubly superb.<br /><br />Again, while I don't feel Rome holds up to repeated viewing, this series is pretty amazing nonetheless. If all films were of this caliber, it would be a very different world.
0316010669|3.0|This book is enjoyable, like all of Malcolm Gadwell's writing, but some organizational problems torpedo the main message. It's NOT to trust your instincts; in fact it's almost the reverse. It's that a few key data points can help you make a decision, provided that you have identified those data points through good research or you have enough experience to know what they are. And often the data points that really matter are not obvious.  For example the coke/pepsi chapter shows that first-glance instincts tell a non-expert that Pepsi is better than Coke, because a non-expert doesn't understand that in the long run, sweetness will reduce enjoyability. The art experts are able to identify a fake on instinct only because they have a huge set of experience that gave them the ability to figure out what information actually matters, not because instincts matter more than logical analysis.<br /><br />The problem is that this point (find out what data points ACTUALLY matter and then you can make quick, good decisions) is not explicitly stated. I blame a lack of good editing, or sloppiness in a rush to publication, or a desire to being the book with a catchy story even though it misleads. It would have been much better to launch and frame the story around the heart attack data---experts thought that age, gender, smoking, etc. matter most in detecting a real heart attack when they didn't.

1847665691|5.0|[[VIDEOID:moI7060DNTLAS2]]LIMITED PARTNERSHIPS AND FIRMS EXPLAINED - AND BY THE WAY, WHAT EXACTLY IS A `PARTNER?'<br /><br />An appreciation by Phillip Taylor MBE and Elizabeth Taylor of Richmond Green Chambers<br /><br />Clarification is the aim and raison d'tre of this superb work of reference which is not only thorough and scholarly but readable too and, therefore, of immense value to practitioners or their clients involved with LLPs, limited partnerships and firms.<br /><br />The nature of these entities can be complex if not confusing.  We don't know that many lay people who realize - as author Mark Blackett-Ord remarks in the Preface - that<br />LLPs (limited liability partnerships) are not `partnerships', nor do they have `limited liability.'<br /><br />The fact that they are generally tax free and that they generally shield their members against negligence claims is what makes them popular. The legislature applies most of the provisions of the Companies Act 2006 to them, but the judiciary treats them as partnerships.  Confused?  You won't be when you read this book. In this the latest edition, published recently by Bloomsbury Professional, these points are clarified in the relevant chapters, namely Chapters 24 and 25.<br /><br />Certainly the very nomenclature inherent this fascinating area of law inevitably generates confusion, as certain terms have shifted in meaning over the years.  A `firm', for example, has a specific meaning (clarified in this book), but newspaper editors and other media folk, for reasons of convenience and space probably, are using it to describe just about any kind of `company', including PLCs.<br /><br />As for the word `partner,' the authors seem resigned to modern realities, if somewhat annoyed by them,  remarking that when the first edition of the book was written, the term \\"partner\\" meant `someone with whom you did business, not someone you went to bed with.'<br /><br />In the brief, but intriguing `Other Entities Akin to Partnerships' section of the book, the sub-section on `Marriage; boyfriends and girlfriends states unambiguously that `unmarried couples are commonly called `partners', but their personal relationship does not make them partners in the legal sense.'<br /><br />Well, point clarified here -- and point taken; another example in our view, of precise and analytical legal minds striving for clarity, while the public, press and media of course, cheerfully and heedlessly muddy the waters.<br /><br />We mention all this merely to impart a flavour of this wide-ranging work of scholarship,(of almost 1,000 pages)  with its extensive coverage of every aspect of partnership law, as a quick trawl through the table of contents --  and the index of over 60 pages will reveal.  Research resources abound, including almost 100 pages of tables of statutes, statutory instruments and cases, together with ten appendices.<br /><br />The law, `stated in our opinion only,' as the author puts it, `is intended to be as at 1 November 2011.'<br /><br />We can't resist quoting what the author has quoted from the translators of the King James Bible (1611) about the savagery of public comment if you put anything noteworthy before the public at all:<br /><br />`Whosoever attempteth any thing for the public...the same setteth himself upon a stage to be gloated upon by every evil eye, yea, he casteth himself headlong upon pikes, to be gored by every sharp tongue.'d girlfriends states unambiguously that `unmarried couples are commonly called `partners', but their personal relationship does not make them partners in the legal sense.' We can't resist quoting what the author has quoted from the translators of the King James Bible (1611) about the savagery of public comment if you put anything noteworthy before the public at all:  `Whosoever attempteth any thing for the public...the same setteth himself upon a stage to be gloated upon by every evil eye, yea, he casteth himself headlong upon pikes, to be gored by every sharp tongue.'
0060555661|5.0|Similar to the bible in that there are a few technical parts that haven't dated well but the overall message sheds light on human behavior and provides powerful, specific guidance on how to act that is just as relevant today as it was at writing. Zweig artfully ties Graham's principals to recent events and defends value investing in modern times.<br /><br />Graham's central thesis is as follows:<br /><br />An investors main goal should be to not LOSE money; To do this one must understand the distinction between 'investing' and 'speculating' and avoid the latter. Most so-called investors including a large proportion of professional fund managers are, on closer inspection, speculators who invest with little knowledge of fundamental value and a slim-to-negative margin of safety.<br /><br />Unfortunately, those trying to get rich quick without paying heed to fundamentals lose over the long term as major losses wipe out short term gains.  Graham introduces a few basic filters and analytical methods to assist in picking safe \\"value\\" stocks which should help the investor to avoid big losses and generate superior returns.<br /><br />Throughout the book, Graham explains where most investors go wrong and with what forms of temptation one must continually deal.  Indeed he does this at such great length that some might find the book boring and long-winded.  However, in my opinion it is time well spent as the ability to maintain process discipline is the biggest differentiator between investors over the long term.  Maybe I'm more interested than most, but for me, it was a page turner.

1400067936|3.0|Nassim Taleb has to be a strong contender for the title of Most Pretentious Author of the 21st century. 0743497465|5.0|This is a typical Dan Brown book in that all the action takes place over the course of 24 hours or so. Multiple crazy conspiracies are discovered; the protagonists get in the way of the evil people; and all is restored at the end. In this case, NASA is in play as the President of the U.S. wants to save it, while his opponent in the next election is running on a platform of extinguishing it. The book starts with a NASA discovery that could make all the billions of dollars spent on it over the years worthwhile. To ensure that this triumph is accepted by all, the President picks five people not known to be part of his campaign and with impeccable (for the most part) credentials to authenticate the discovery. The \\"evil genius\\" will stop at nothing to achieve his or her aims and the game, as they say, is afoot.<br /><br />While this book is big and long at 530+ pages, it is well written and the pages kept getting flipped very quickly. It helps that the plot is very engaging as are some of the characters that are brought to life. Oh, not everything is rosy. There are various places where you have to go \\"huh?\\" and there are several plot points that are clearly not connected, but who cares? It's still a thrilling adventure tale told with gusto and flashes of humor. Being a fan of thrillers of this type, I quite quickly identified who the culprits are likely to be and what the real deception is. However, even I was taken completely by surprise when the real \\"evil genius\\" was identified. Not what I expected at all and therefore kudos to Dan Brown!<br /><br />With any of these thrillers one must suspend one's disbelief and simply take it all in. The author does a good job of providing all kinds of scientific interludes in which he attempts to explain various phenomena. I am not a scientist and cannot vouch for whether any of these scientific explanations are plausible or even real, but I simply took them at face value and accepted them. If the killers succeeded after all, the story would not have ended as it should.<br /><br />So, overall this book is an absolute winner and I would recommend it to anyone who likes the genre or needs something to occupy their minds. B005SFR7YE|5.0|I have been in love with Phantom since I was 14. I've seen the stage production live 3 times, I read Gaston Leroux's novel, I read Susan Kay's novel, I have the original Broadway cast recording memorized, and when the movie came out in 2004, I was utterly disappointed at what a lousy hack job it was. Living in Hickville, Florida means that the live production doesn't come around very often, and I was hoping the movie would be a suitable substitution. It definitely wasn't -- but THIS IS!!<br /><br />I thought that this was something like Les Mis in Concert -- the performers are in costume and there is some staging, and that's it. This is actually a recording of the entire musical from start to finish. There are different camera angles and occasional video editing effects to spice it up. Otherwise, this is like sitting in the orchestra section on opening night.<br /><br />All of the cast members are phenomenal. One of the biggest problems with the movie is that the words were there, but there was no emotion. Not so in this production. I honestly think that the roles of the Phantom and Christine are among the most difficult Broadway roles to perform well, not only because of the range and other vocal demands, but because of the huge range of emotions that each has to take on in a 2 1/2 hour period. Both performers were absolutely phenomenal in bringing their characters to life.<br /><br />There are two scenes that have huge significance to me: Music of the Night, and the graveyard scene. I suffered through Butler's performance of MotN once, and only once. I refuse to ever sit through it again because of how bad it was. Ramin Karimloo not only has a seductive, velvet-like voice, but he also portrays the internal struggle of the Phantom entirely through his subtle actions and facial expressions. He doesn't just stand stiffly and stare at Christine. Now, the graveyard scene has been botched both in the movie and in the theater. \\"Wishing You Were Somehow Here Again\\" is hugely personal to me, because I lost my father when I was 9. I know what it feels like to stand at my father's grave and want to move on but be afraid of letting go. I absolutely hate when actresses just stand there with no emotion except a sullen pout and maybe just walk around. At the end of the song, you can see that Sierra Boggess actually has tears in her eyes. She gives the song all of the fear, the anger, the desperation, and the hope that it is meant to have. During \\"Wandering Child,\\" there is so much intensity between the Phantom, Raoul, and Christine. I hate when directors cut it down to a duet and the harmony from Raoul's part is missing. I was thrilled to see this song performed in all its glory.<br /><br />The icing on the cake is the grand finale. I won't spoil it, because that was a wonderful surprise for me, but don't turn off the DVD player after the bows. :)lost my father when I was 9. I won't spoil it, because that was a wonderful surprise for me, but don't turn off the DVD player after the bows. :)
